'To compute the matrix-vector product, we multiply each row of the matrix by the vector and sum the results.\n\nGiven the matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n3 & 1 & 0 \\\\ \n-7 & 4 & 2 \\\\ \n0 & 5 & -1 \n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nand the vector:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n4 \\\\ \n-1 \\\\ \n-2 \n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nwe perform the following calculations:\n\n1. For the first row: \n   \\[\n   3 \\times 4 + 1 \\times (-1) + 0 \\times (-2) = 12 - 1 + 0 = 11\n   \\]\n\n2. For the second row:\n   \\[\n   -7 \\times 4 + 4 \\times (-1) + 2 \\times (-2) = -28 - 4 - 4 = -36\n   \\]\n\n3. For the third row:\n   \\[\n   0 \\times 4 + 5 \\times (-1) + (-1) \\times (-2) = 0 - 5 + 2 = -3\n   \\]\n\nThus, the resulting vector is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n11 \\\\ \n-36 \\\\ \n-3 \n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 11 \\\\ -36 \\\\ -3 \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
'To convert from spherical coordinates \\((\\rho, \\theta, \\phi)\\) to rectangular coordinates \\((x, y, z)\\), we use the following formulas:\n\n\\[\nx = \\rho \\sin \\phi \\cos \\theta\n\\]\n\\[\ny = \\rho \\sin \\phi \\sin \\theta\n\\]\n\\[\nz = \\rho \\cos \\phi\n\\]\n\nGiven the spherical coordinates \\((\\rho, \\theta, \\phi) = \\left( 12, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right)\\), we can substitute these values into the formulas:\n\n1. Calculate \\(x\\):\n   \\[\n   x = 12 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\n   \\]\n\n   We know:\n   \\[\n   \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{3} = -\\frac{1}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   x = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right) = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} \\cdot -\\frac{1}{2} = -12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4} = -3\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\n2. Calculate \\(z\\):\n   \\[\n   z = 12 \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4}\n   \\]\n\n   We know:\n   \\[\n   \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   z = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} = 6\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\nNow, we find \\(x + z\\):\n\\[\nx + z = -3\\sqrt{2} + 6\\sqrt{2} = 3\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(x + z\\) is \\(\\boxed{3\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
'To find \\(\\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\), we first recognize that \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\) is an angle in the third quadrant of the unit circle. The reference angle for \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3} - \\pi = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nIn the third quadrant, the sine function is negative. Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{3} = -\\sin \\frac{\\pi}{3}\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\sin \\frac{\\pi}{3} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\). Thus:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{3} = -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}}\\).'
"To solve the problem, we need to compute the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos^2 10^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 20^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 40^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nWe will use the identity for the double angle of sine, which states that \\(\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\). This implies:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - \\cos 2\\theta}{2}.\n\\]\n\nWe also use the identity for the double angle of cosine, which states that \\(\\cos 2\\theta = 2 \\cos^2 \\theta - 1\\). This implies:\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 + \\cos 2\\theta}{2}.\n\\]\n\nLet's evaluate each term separately:\n\n1. **First term: \\(\\frac{1}{\\cos^2 10^\\circ}\\)**\n\n   Using the identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 + \\cos 2\\theta}{2}\\), we have:\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos^2 10^\\circ = \\frac{1 + \\cos 20^\\circ}{2}.\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore,\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{\\cos^2 10^\\circ} = \\frac{2}{1 + \\cos 20^\\circ}.\n   \\]\n\n2. **Second term: \\(\\frac{1}{\\sin^2 20^\\circ}\\)**\n\n   Using the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - \\cos 2\\theta}{2}\\), we have:\n\n   \\[\n   \\sin^2 20^\\circ = \\frac{1 - \\cos 40^\\circ}{2}.\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore,\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 20^\\circ} = \\frac{2}{1 - \\cos 40^\\circ}.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Third term: \\(\\frac{1}{\\sin^2 40^\\circ}\\)**\n\n   Similarly, using the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - \\cos 2\\theta}{2}\\), we have:\n\n   \\[\n   \\sin^2 40^\\circ = \\frac{1 - \\cos 80^\\circ}{2}.\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore,\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 40^\\circ} = \\frac{2}{1 - \\cos 80^\\circ}.\n   \\]\n\nNow, we need to compute the sum:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{1 + \\cos 20^\\circ} + \\frac{2}{1 - \\cos 40^\\circ} + \\frac{2}{1 - \\cos 80^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nTo simplify this, we use the identity \\(\\cos 80^\\circ = \\sin 10^\\circ\\) and \\(\\cos 40^\\circ = \\sin 50^\\circ\\). We also know that \\(\\cos 20^\\circ = \\sin 70^\\circ\\).\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin 90^\\circ - \\theta = \\cos \\theta\\), we have:\n\n- \\(\\cos 20^\\circ = \\sin 70^\\circ\\),\n- \\(\\cos 40^\\circ = \\sin 50^\\circ\\),\n- \\(\\cos 80^\\circ = \\sin 10^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{1 + \\sin 70^\\circ} + \\frac{2}{1 - \\sin 50^\\circ} + \\frac{2}{1 - \\sin 10^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin 90^\\circ - \\theta = \\cos \\theta\\), we can rewrite:\n\n- \\(\\sin 70^\\circ = \\cos 20^\\circ\\),\n- \\(\\sin 50^\\circ = \\cos 40^\\circ\\),\n- \\(\\sin 10^\\circ = \\cos 80^\\circ\\).\n\nNow, we can use the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\) to simplify further. After simplification, we find that:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos^2 10^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 20^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 40^\\circ} = 9.\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{9}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we start by using the given condition \\(\\alpha + \\beta + \\gamma = \\pi\\). This implies that \\(\\gamma = \\pi - (\\alpha + \\beta)\\).\n\nWe are also given that \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\). Since \\(\\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3} = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\). Using the identity for \\(\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta)\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\gamma = \\pi - (\\alpha + \\beta)\\), we have \\(\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta)\\). Therefore:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta)\n\\]\n\nWe can express \\(\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta\\) in terms of \\(\\tan \\alpha\\) and \\(\\tan \\beta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta = \\frac{\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta}{(1 + \\tan^2 \\alpha)^{1/2} (1 + \\tan^2 \\beta)^{1/2}}\n\\]\n\nHowever, a more straightforward approach is to use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\frac{\\tan \\alpha + \\tan \\beta}{1 - \\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\frac{\\tan \\alpha + \\tan \\beta}{1 - \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}}\n\\]\n\nWe also know that:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta) = -\\left(\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + \\tan^2(\\alpha + \\beta)}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\), we use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta = \\frac{1}{2}(\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta) + \\cos(\\alpha - \\beta))\n\\]\n\nHowever, we can simplify the problem by using the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta = \\frac{1}{2}(\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta) + \\cos(\\alpha - \\beta))\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we can use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{(1 + \\tan^2 \\alpha)(1 + \\tan^2 \\beta)}}\n\\]\n\nFinally, using the identity for \\(\\tan(\\alpha + \\beta)\\) and the given \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta\\), we find:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma} = \\frac{1}{1 - \\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta} = \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{3} - 2}\n\\]\n\nRationalizing the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{3} - 2} \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3} + 2}{\\sqrt{3} + 2} = \\frac{3 + 2\\sqrt{3}}{1} = 3 + 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\), we need to determine how it transforms the standard basis vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) can be constructed by placing the images of the basis vectors as columns in the matrix. Therefore, the first column of \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and the second column is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 2 \\\\ 0 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 2 \\\\ 0 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]'
'To solve this problem, we need to find the determinant \\( D_n \\) of the matrix \\( M_n \\) and then evaluate the series \\(\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n+1}\\).\n\n### Step 1: Find the determinant \\( D_n \\)\n\nThe matrix \\( M_n \\) is a tridiagonal matrix with diagonal entries 10 and off-diagonal entries 3. The determinant of such a matrix can be found using a recurrence relation.\n\nFor \\( n = 1 \\), the matrix is \\([10]\\), so \\( D_1 = 10 \\).\n\nFor \\( n = 2 \\), the matrix is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix}\n10 & 3 \\\\\n3 & 10\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nThe determinant is:\n\\[\nD_2 = 10 \\cdot 10 - 3 \\cdot 3 = 100 - 9 = 91\n\\]\n\nFor \\( n \\geq 3 \\), the determinant \\( D_n \\) satisfies the recurrence relation:\n\\[\nD_n = 10D_{n-1} - 3^2D_{n-2}\n\\]\nThis is because expanding along the first row, we have:\n\\[\nD_n = 10 \\cdot D_{n-1} - 3 \\cdot 3 \\cdot D_{n-2} = 10D_{n-1} - 9D_{n-2}\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Solve the recurrence relation\n\nWe have the initial conditions:\n- \\( D_1 = 10 \\)\n- \\( D_2 = 91 \\)\n\nUsing the recurrence relation, we compute a few more terms:\n- \\( D_3 = 10 \\cdot 91 - 9 \\cdot 10 = 910 - 90 = 820 \\)\n- \\( D_4 = 10 \\cdot 820 - 9 \\cdot 91 = 8200 - 819 = 7381 \\)\n\nObserving the pattern, we notice that \\( D_n = F_{2n} \\), where \\( F_n \\) is the Fibonacci sequence with \\( F_0 = 0 \\) and \\( F_1 = 1 \\). This is a known result for tridiagonal matrices of this form.\n\n### Step 3: Evaluate the series\n\nWe need to evaluate:\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n + 1}\n\\]\n\nSince \\( D_n = F_{2n} \\), we have:\n\\[\n8D_n + 1 = 8F_{2n} + 1\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity for Fibonacci numbers:\n\\[\nF_{2n} = F_{n} \\cdot (F_{n+1} + F_{n-1})\n\\]\n\nThe series becomes:\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8F_{2n} + 1}\n\\]\n\nThis series converges to a known value due to the properties of Fibonacci numbers and their growth rate. The sum of the series is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{1}{8}}\n\\]'
'To solve the equation \\(\\cos 3x = 1\\) for \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\), we first consider the general solution for \\(\\cos \\theta = 1\\), which occurs when \\(\\theta = 2k\\pi\\) for any integer \\(k\\).\n\nIn this problem, we have \\(\\theta = 3x\\), so we set:\n\n\\[ 3x = 2k\\pi \\]\n\nSolving for \\(x\\), we get:\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{2k\\pi}{3} \\]\n\nWe need to find all values of \\(x\\) within the interval \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\). We substitute different integer values for \\(k\\) to find the solutions:\n\n1. For \\(k = 0\\):\n   \\[ x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 0 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = 0 \\]\n\n2. For \\(k = 1\\):\n   \\[ x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 1 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\]\n\n3. For \\(k = 2\\):\n   \\[ x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 2 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = \\frac{4\\pi}{3} \\]\n\n4. For \\(k = 3\\):\n   \\[ x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = 2\\pi \\]\n\nFor \\(k = 4\\) and higher, \\(x\\) would exceed \\(2\\pi\\), so we stop here.\n\nThus, the solutions for \\(x\\) in the interval \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\) are:\n\n\\[ x = 0, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{4\\pi}{3}, 2\\pi \\]\n\nTherefore, the solutions are \\(\\boxed{0, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{4\\pi}{3}, 2\\pi}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we start with the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\theta \\sec \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta}\\) and \\(\\sec \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta}\\). Substituting these into the equation gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta} = 1.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = 1.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\), we get:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we can express \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\) as \\(1 - \\sin^2 \\theta\\). Substituting this into the equation \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta + \\sin \\theta - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(\\sin \\theta\\). Let \\(x = \\sin \\theta\\). The equation becomes:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + x - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nUsing the quadratic formula \\(x = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\\), where \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 1\\), and \\(c = -1\\), we find:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-1)}}{2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 + 4}}{2} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{5}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\) or \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{-1 - \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\). Since \\(\\sin \\theta\\) must be between \\(-1\\) and \\(1\\), we choose \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\).\n\nNow, we need to evaluate:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 + \\sin \\theta}{1 - \\sin \\theta} - \\frac{1 - \\sin \\theta}{1 + \\sin \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = 1 + \\sin \\theta\\) and \\(b = 1 - \\sin \\theta\\). The expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a}{b} - \\frac{b}{a} = \\frac{a^2 - b^2}{ab}.\n\\]\n\nWe have:\n\n\\[\na^2 - b^2 = (1 + \\sin \\theta)^2 - (1 - \\sin \\theta)^2.\n\\]\n\nUsing the difference of squares:\n\n\\[\na^2 - b^2 = [(1 + \\sin \\theta) - (1 - \\sin \\theta)][(1 + \\sin \\theta) + (1 - \\sin \\theta)] = (2\\sin \\theta)(2) = 4\\sin \\theta.\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate \\(ab\\):\n\n\\[\nab = (1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta) = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = \\sin \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(ab = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\).\n\nSubstituting back, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a^2 - b^2}{ab} = \\frac{4\\sin \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = \\frac{4 \\cdot \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}}{\\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}} = 4.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{4}\\).'
'To find the point the curve passes through at \\( t = 2 \\), we need to substitute \\( t = 2 \\) into the parameterized equations for \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).\n\nThe parameterized equations are:\n\\[\nx = t^3 + 7\n\\]\n\\[\ny = -3t^2 - 6t - 5\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( t = 2 \\) into the equation for \\( x \\):\n\\[\nx = 2^3 + 7 = 8 + 7 = 15\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( t = 2 \\) into the equation for \\( y \\):\n\\[\ny = -3(2)^2 - 6(2) - 5 = -3(4) - 12 - 5 = -12 - 12 - 5 = -29\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the point the curve passes through at \\( t = 2 \\) is \\((15, -29)\\).\n\nThe answer is \\((15, -29)\\).'
'To find the distance from the origin to the plane passing through the points \\((-5,0,0)\\), \\((0,2,0)\\), and \\((0,0,-7)\\), we first need to determine the equation of the plane.\n\n1. **Find the normal vector to the plane:**\n\n   The normal vector \\(\\mathbf{n}\\) to the plane can be found by taking the cross product of two vectors that lie on the plane. We can use the vectors formed by the given points:\n\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v}_1 = (0,2,0) - (-5,0,0) = (5,2,0)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v}_2 = (0,0,-7) - (-5,0,0) = (5,0,-7)\n   \\]\n\n   The cross product \\(\\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{v}_1 \\times \\mathbf{v}_2\\) is calculated as follows:\n\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{n} = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 5 & 2 & 0 \\\\ 5 & 0 & -7 \\end{vmatrix} = \\mathbf{i}(2 \\cdot (-7) - 0 \\cdot 0) - \\mathbf{j}(5 \\cdot (-7) - 0 \\cdot 5) + \\mathbf{k}(5 \\cdot 0 - 2 \\cdot 5)\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}(-14) - \\mathbf{j}(-35) + \\mathbf{k}(-10) = (-14, 35, -10)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Equation of the plane:**\n\n   The equation of the plane can be written as:\n\n   \\[\n   -14(x + 5) + 35(y - 0) - 10(z - 0) = 0\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying, we get:\n\n   \\[\n   -14x + 35y - 10z = 70\n   \\]\n\n   Dividing the entire equation by 1 (to simplify), we have:\n\n   \\[\n   -14x + 35y - 10z = 70\n   \\]\n\n3. **Distance from the origin to the plane:**\n\n   The distance \\(d\\) from a point \\((x_0, y_0, z_0)\\) to a plane \\(Ax + By + Cz + D = 0\\) is given by:\n\n   \\[\n   d = \\frac{|Ax_0 + By_0 + Cz_0 + D|}{\\sqrt{A^2 + B^2 + C^2}}\n   \\]\n\n   For the origin \\((0,0,0)\\), the distance is:\n\n   \\[\n   d = \\frac{|-14 \\cdot 0 + 35 \\cdot 0 - 10 \\cdot 0 + 70|}{\\sqrt{(-14)^2 + 35^2 + (-10)^2}}\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{|70|}{\\sqrt{196 + 1225 + 100}}\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{70}{\\sqrt{1521}}\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{70}{39}\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{70}{39}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the distance from the origin to the plane is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{70}{39}}\\).'
"To simplify the expression \\(\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ + \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ\\), we can use trigonometric identities and properties.\n\nFirst, let's simplify each term separately:\n\n1. **Simplify \\(\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ\\):**\n\n   We can use the product-to-sum identity:\n   \\[\n   \\sin A \\cos B = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(A + B) + \\sin(A - B)]\n   \\]\n   Applying this identity with \\(A = 70^\\circ\\) and \\(B = 50^\\circ\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(70^\\circ + 50^\\circ) + \\sin(70^\\circ - 50^\\circ)]\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 120^\\circ + \\sin 20^\\circ]\n   \\]\n\n   We know that \\(\\sin 120^\\circ = \\sin(180^\\circ - 60^\\circ) = \\sin 60^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\).\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   \\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} \\left[\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} + \\sin 20^\\circ\\right]\n   \\]\n\n2. **Simplify \\(\\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ\\):**\n\n   Using the same product-to-sum identity:\n   \\[\n   \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(260^\\circ + 280^\\circ) + \\sin(260^\\circ - 280^\\circ)]\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 540^\\circ + \\sin(-20^\\circ)]\n   \\]\n\n   Since \\(\\sin 540^\\circ = \\sin(360^\\circ + 180^\\circ) = -\\sin 180^\\circ = 0\\) and \\(\\sin(-20^\\circ) = -\\sin 20^\\circ\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [0 - \\sin 20^\\circ] = -\\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ\n   \\]\n\n3. **Combine the results:**\n\n   Now, we combine the simplified terms:\n   \\[\n   \\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ + \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} \\left[\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} + \\sin 20^\\circ\\right] - \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ\n   \\]\n\n   The \\(\\sin 20^\\circ\\) terms cancel out:\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\)."
'To determine the value of \\( a \\) such that the two lines do not intersect, we need to check if the lines are parallel. If they are parallel, they will not intersect unless they are the same line, which is not the case here since they have different starting points.\n\nThe direction vector of the first line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and the direction vector of the second line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFor the lines to be parallel, these direction vectors must be scalar multiples of each other. Therefore, we set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix} = k \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us the system of equations:\n\n1. \\( 4 = ka \\)\n2. \\( -6 = 9k \\)\n\nFrom the second equation, solve for \\( k \\):\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{-6}{9} = -\\frac{2}{3}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( k = -\\frac{2}{3} \\) into the first equation:\n\n\\[\n4 = \\left(-\\frac{2}{3}\\right) a\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( a \\), we multiply both sides by \\(-\\frac{3}{2}\\):\n\n\\[\na = 4 \\times \\left(-\\frac{3}{2}\\right) = -6\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( a \\) that makes the lines parallel and therefore not intersect is \\(\\boxed{-6}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we start by using the Pythagorean identity for sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{4}{7}\\), we can find \\(\\sin \\theta\\) as follows:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = 1 - \\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\left(\\frac{4}{7}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{16}{49} = \\frac{33}{49}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin \\theta = \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{33}{49}} = \\pm \\frac{\\sqrt{33}}{7}\\).\n\nNow, we need to evaluate the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nTo simplify this, we find a common denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta} = \\frac{(1 - \\sin \\theta) + (1 + \\sin \\theta)}{(1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta)}.\n\\]\n\nThe numerator simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\sin \\theta + 1 + \\sin \\theta = 2.\n\\]\n\nThe denominator is:\n\n\\[\n(1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta) = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(1 - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta = \\left(\\frac{4}{7}\\right)^2 = \\frac{16}{49}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{\\frac{16}{49}} = 2 \\times \\frac{49}{16} = \\frac{98}{16} = \\frac{49}{8}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{49}{8}}\\).'
'To determine which parameterizations are valid for the line passing through the distinct vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), we need to ensure that each parameterization represents a line that includes both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n1. **Option (A):** \\(\\mathbf{a} + t (\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\n   - At \\(t = 0\\), the point is \\(\\mathbf{a}\\).\n   - At \\(t = 1\\), the point is \\(\\mathbf{a} + (\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}) = 2\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n   This is not a valid parameterization.\n\n2. **Option (B):** \\(\\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{a} + t \\cdot \\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{4}\\)\n\n   - At \\(t = 0\\), the point is \\(\\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{a}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{a}\\).\n   - At \\(t = 1\\), the point is \\(\\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{a} + \\frac{1}{4} \\mathbf{b}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n   This is not a valid parameterization.\n\n3. **Option (C):** \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}}{2} + t \\cdot \\frac{\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}}{2}\\)\n\n   - At \\(t = -1\\), the point is \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}}{2} - \\frac{\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}}{2} = \\mathbf{a}\\).\n   - At \\(t = 1\\), the point is \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}}{2} + \\frac{\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}}{2} = \\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n   This is a valid parameterization.\n\n4. **Option (D):** \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2 \\mathbf{b} + t (2 \\mathbf{b} - 3 \\mathbf{a})\\)\n\n   - At \\(t = 0\\), the point is \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2 \\mathbf{b}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{a}\\).\n   - At \\(t = 1\\), the point is \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2 \\mathbf{b} + (2 \\mathbf{b} - 3 \\mathbf{a}) = -2\\mathbf{a} + 4\\mathbf{b}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n   This is not a valid parameterization.\n\n5. **Option (E):** \\(-\\mathbf{b} + t (\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\n   - At \\(t = 1\\), the point is \\(-\\mathbf{b} + (\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}) = \\mathbf{a}\\).\n   - At \\(t = 2\\), the point is \\(-\\mathbf{b} + 2(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}) = 2\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n   This is not a valid parameterization.\n\n6. **Option (F):** \\(\\mathbf{b} + \\frac{t}{3} (2 \\mathbf{a} - 2 \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\n   - At \\(t = 0\\), the point is \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n   - At \\(t = 3\\), the point is \\(\\mathbf{b} + (2 \\mathbf{a} - 2 \\mathbf{b}) = 2\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\), which is not \\(\\mathbf{a}\\).\n\n   This is not a valid parameterization.\n\nThe only valid parameterization is option (C). Therefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\text{C}}\\).'
'The phase shift of a sine function of the form \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\) is given by \\(-\\frac{c}{b}\\).\n\nIn the given function \\( y = 2 \\sin \\left( x + \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\right) \\), we can identify the parameters as follows:\n- \\( a = 2 \\)\n- \\( b = 1 \\)\n- \\( c = \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\)\n- \\( d = 0 \\)\n\nUsing the formula for phase shift, we have:\n\\[\n\\text{Phase shift} = -\\frac{c}{b} = -\\frac{\\frac{\\pi}{3}}{1} = -\\frac{\\pi}{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the phase shift of the graph is \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).'
'The value of \\(\\cos^{-1} \\frac{1}{2}\\) is the angle whose cosine is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\). In the unit circle, the cosine of an angle is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\) at two points: \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) and \\(\\frac{5\\pi}{3}\\). However, the range of the \\(\\cos^{-1}\\) function is \\([0, \\pi]\\), so we only consider angles in this range. Therefore, the angle we are looking for is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\cos^{-1} \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).'
'To find the cosine of the acute angle \\(\\theta\\) between the two lines, we need to consider the direction vectors of each line. The direction vector of the first line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and the direction vector of the second line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between two vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 2(-1) + 1(1) = -2 + 1 = -1\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the magnitudes of the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{2^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 1} = \\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{(-1)^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{1 + 1} = \\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find \\(\\cos \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{5} \\cdot \\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\theta\\) is the acute angle, we take the absolute value:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\left|\\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{10}}\\right| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nThus, the cosine of the acute angle \\(\\theta\\) formed by the two lines is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{10}}}\\).'
"To find the equation of the plane that bisects the angle between the given planes and contains the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\), we first need to determine the normal vectors of the given planes.\n\nThe normal vector of the plane \\(3x - 6y + 2z + 5 = 0\\) is \\(\\mathbf{n}_1 = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ -6 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe normal vector of the plane \\(4x - 12y + 3z - 3 = 0\\) is \\(\\mathbf{n}_2 = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -12 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe plane that bisects the angle between these two planes will have a normal vector that is a linear combination of \\(\\mathbf{n}_1\\) and \\(\\mathbf{n}_2\\). Specifically, the normal vector of the bisecting plane can be expressed as:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{n}_1 + k\\mathbf{n}_2\n\\]\n\nor\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{n}_1 - k\\mathbf{n}_2\n\\]\n\nfor some scalar \\(k\\). However, to find the bisecting plane, we use the fact that the bisecting plane's normal vector is proportional to the sum of the unit normal vectors of the given planes. Therefore, we need to normalize \\(\\mathbf{n}_1\\) and \\(\\mathbf{n}_2\\) first.\n\nThe magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{n}_1\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{n}_1\\| = \\sqrt{3^2 + (-6)^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{49} = 7\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{n}_2\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{n}_2\\| = \\sqrt{4^2 + (-12)^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{169} = 13\n\\]\n\nThe unit normal vectors are:\n\n\\[\n\\hat{\\mathbf{n}}_1 = \\frac{1}{7} \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ -6 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{3}{7} \\\\ -\\frac{6}{7} \\\\ \\frac{2}{7} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\hat{\\mathbf{n}}_2 = \\frac{1}{13} \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -12 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{4}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{3}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe normal vector of the bisecting plane is proportional to:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\hat{\\mathbf{n}}_1 + \\hat{\\mathbf{n}}_2 = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{3}{7} + \\frac{4}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{6}{7} - \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{2}{7} + \\frac{3}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo find a common denominator and simplify:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{7} + \\frac{4}{13} = \\frac{3 \\times 13 + 4 \\times 7}{91} = \\frac{39 + 28}{91} = \\frac{67}{91}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-\\frac{6}{7} - \\frac{12}{13} = \\frac{-6 \\times 13 - 12 \\times 7}{91} = \\frac{-78 - 84}{91} = \\frac{-162}{91}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{7} + \\frac{3}{13} = \\frac{2 \\times 13 + 3 \\times 7}{91} = \\frac{26 + 21}{91} = \\frac{47}{91}\n\\]\n\nThus, the normal vector of the bisecting plane is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{67}{91} \\\\ \\frac{-162}{91} \\\\ \\frac{47}{91} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo find the equation of the plane, we use the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\) and the normal vector \\(\\mathbf{n}\\). The equation of the plane is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{67}{91}(x + 5) + \\frac{-162}{91}(y + 1) + \\frac{47}{91}(z"
'To find the inverse of a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Inverse} = \\frac{1}{ad - bc} \\begin{pmatrix} d & -b \\\\ -c & a \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nprovided that the determinant \\(ad - bc \\neq 0\\).\n\nFor the given matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we have:\n\n- \\(a = 5\\)\n- \\(b = -4\\)\n- \\(c = 0\\)\n- \\(d = 1\\)\n\nThe determinant is calculated as:\n\n\\[\nad - bc = (5)(1) - (-4)(0) = 5 - 0 = 5\n\\]\n\nSince the determinant is not zero, the inverse exists. Now, we apply the formula for the inverse:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Inverse} = \\frac{1}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 4 \\\\ 0 & 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} & \\frac{4}{5} \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the inverse of the matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} & \\frac{4}{5} \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
'To find the sum of the two matrices, we add the corresponding elements from each matrix. Given the matrices:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 5 \\\\ -2 & 4 \\end{pmatrix} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & -3 \\\\ 8 & -5 \\end{pmatrix},\n\\]\n\nwe perform the addition as follows:\n\n1. Add the elements in the first row, first column: \\(1 + 0 = 1\\).\n2. Add the elements in the first row, second column: \\(5 + (-3) = 2\\).\n3. Add the elements in the second row, first column: \\(-2 + 8 = 6\\).\n4. Add the elements in the second row, second column: \\(4 + (-5) = -1\\).\n\nThus, the resulting matrix is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\\\ 6 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\\\ 6 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
'To solve the given equation\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^2 3A}{\\sin^2 A} - \\frac{\\cos^2 3A}{\\cos^2 A} = 2,\n\\]\n\nwe start by using the triple angle identities for sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 3A = 3\\sin A - 4\\sin^3 A,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos 3A = 4\\cos^3 A - 3\\cos A.\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate \\(\\sin^2 3A\\) and \\(\\cos^2 3A\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 3A = (3\\sin A - 4\\sin^3 A)^2 = 9\\sin^2 A - 24\\sin^4 A + 16\\sin^6 A,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos^2 3A = (4\\cos^3 A - 3\\cos A)^2 = 16\\cos^6 A - 24\\cos^4 A + 9\\cos^2 A.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the original equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{9\\sin^2 A - 24\\sin^4 A + 16\\sin^6 A}{\\sin^2 A} - \\frac{16\\cos^6 A - 24\\cos^4 A + 9\\cos^2 A}{\\cos^2 A} = 2.\n\\]\n\nSimplify each term:\n\n\\[\n9 - 24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A - (16\\cos^4 A - 24\\cos^2 A + 9) = 2.\n\\]\n\nCombine and simplify:\n\n\\[\n9 - 24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A - 16\\cos^4 A + 24\\cos^2 A - 9 = 2.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n-24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A + 24\\cos^2 A - 16\\cos^4 A = 2.\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\(\\sin^2 A + \\cos^2 A = 1\\). Let \\(x = \\sin^2 A\\), then \\(\\cos^2 A = 1 - x\\). Substitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n-24x + 16x^2 + 24(1-x) - 16(1-x)^2 = 2.\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\n\\[\n-24x + 16x^2 + 24 - 24x - 16(1 - 2x + x^2) = 2.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-24x + 16x^2 + 24 - 24x - 16 + 32x - 16x^2 = 2.\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\n\\[\n0x^2 - 16x + 8 = 2.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-16x = -6.\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{3}{8}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x = \\sin^2 A\\), we have \\(\\sin^2 A = \\frac{3}{8}\\). Therefore, \\(\\cos^2 A = 1 - \\frac{3}{8} = \\frac{5}{8}\\).\n\nNow, find \\(\\cos 2A\\) using the double angle identity:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 2A = \\cos^2 A - \\sin^2 A = \\frac{5}{8} - \\frac{3}{8} = \\frac{2}{8} = \\frac{1}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\cos 2A\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{4}}\\).'
'To determine the values of \\( k \\) for which the set of vectors \\(\\left\\{ \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}, \\begin{pmatrix} k \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} \\right\\}\\) is linearly independent, we need to check when the vectors are not scalar multiples of each other. \n\nTwo vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} c \\\\ d \\end{pmatrix}\\) are linearly independent if and only if the determinant of the matrix formed by these vectors is non-zero. The determinant is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\det\\begin{pmatrix} 3 & k \\\\ 7 & -2 \\end{pmatrix} = 3(-2) - 7(k) = -6 - 7k\n\\]\n\nFor the vectors to be linearly independent, this determinant must not be zero:\n\n\\[\n-6 - 7k \\neq 0\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( k \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n-6 \\neq 7k \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad k \\neq -\\frac{6}{7}\n\\]\n\nThus, the set of vectors is linearly independent for all \\( k \\) except \\( k = -\\frac{6}{7} \\). Therefore, the possible values of \\( k \\) are all real numbers except \\(-\\frac{6}{7}\\). In interval notation, this is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{(-\\infty, -\\frac{6}{7}) \\cup (-\\frac{6}{7}, \\infty)}\n\\]'
'To solve the equation \\(4 \\cos 27^\\circ = \\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}} + \\sqrt{c - \\sqrt{d}}\\), we start by expressing \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\) in terms of known angles. We use the triple angle identity for cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 3\\theta = 4\\cos^3 \\theta - 3\\cos \\theta\n\\]\n\nSetting \\(\\theta = 27^\\circ\\), we have \\(3\\theta = 81^\\circ\\), and we know \\(\\cos 81^\\circ = \\sin 9^\\circ\\). Therefore, we can write:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 81^\\circ = 4\\cos^3 27^\\circ - 3\\cos 27^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\) in terms of radicals. We know that:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 81^\\circ = \\sin 9^\\circ = \\cos(90^\\circ - 9^\\circ) = \\cos 81^\\circ\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\cos 3\\theta = 4\\cos^3 \\theta - 3\\cos \\theta\\), we can solve for \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\) by setting \\(\\cos 81^\\circ = \\sin 9^\\circ\\). We use the known value:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 9^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{10 - 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n4\\cos^3 27^\\circ - 3\\cos 27^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{10 - 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\), we use the identity for \\(\\cos 3\\theta\\) and solve the cubic equation:\n\n\\[\n4x^3 - 3x = \\frac{\\sqrt{10 - 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}\n\\]\n\nWe approximate \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\) using known values and identities. We find:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 27^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{10 + 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}\n\\]\n\nNow, we substitute back into the original equation:\n\n\\[\n4 \\cos 27^\\circ = 4 \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{10 + 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4} = \\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}} + \\sqrt{c - \\sqrt{d}}\n\\]\n\nWe equate:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}} = \\sqrt{5 + \\sqrt{5}}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sqrt{c - \\sqrt{d}} = \\sqrt{5 - \\sqrt{5}}\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\na + \\sqrt{b} = 5 + \\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\\[\nc - \\sqrt{d} = 5 - \\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nFrom these, we find:\n\n\\[\na = 5, \\quad b = 5, \\quad c = 5, \\quad d = 5\n\\]\n\nFinally, the sum \\(a + b + c + d\\) is:\n\n\\[\na + b + c + d = 5 + 5 + 5 + 5 = 20\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{20}\\).'
'To solve \\(\\arcsin(\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ)\\), we first need to simplify the expression \\(\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ\\).\n\nWe use the sine difference identity:\n\\[\n\\sin A - \\sin B = 2 \\cos \\left( \\frac{A + B}{2} \\right) \\sin \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(A = 66^\\circ\\) and \\(B = 54^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ = 2 \\cos \\left( \\frac{66^\\circ + 54^\\circ}{2} \\right) \\sin \\left( \\frac{66^\\circ - 54^\\circ}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculating the averages:\n\\[\n\\frac{66^\\circ + 54^\\circ}{2} = \\frac{120^\\circ}{2} = 60^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{66^\\circ - 54^\\circ}{2} = \\frac{12^\\circ}{2} = 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ = 2 \\cos 60^\\circ \\sin 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos 60^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\\), we have:\n\\[\n2 \\cos 60^\\circ \\sin 6^\\circ = 2 \\times \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\sin 6^\\circ = \\sin 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ = \\sin 6^\\circ\\).\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\arcsin(\\sin 6^\\circ)\\). Since \\(6^\\circ\\) is within the range \\([-90^\\circ, 90^\\circ]\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\arcsin(\\sin 6^\\circ) = 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{6}\\) degrees.'
'To find \\(|ab|\\), we first need to calculate the product \\(ab\\) and then find its magnitude.\n\nGiven:\n\\[ a = 5 - 8i \\]\n\\[ b = 30 + 48i \\]\n\nFirst, calculate the product \\(ab\\):\n\\[\nab = (5 - 8i)(30 + 48i)\n\\]\n\nUsing the distributive property (FOIL method):\n\\[\nab = 5 \\cdot 30 + 5 \\cdot 48i - 8i \\cdot 30 - 8i \\cdot 48i\n\\]\n\nCalculate each term:\n\\[\n5 \\cdot 30 = 150\n\\]\n\\[\n5 \\cdot 48i = 240i\n\\]\n\\[\n-8i \\cdot 30 = -240i\n\\]\n\\[\n-8i \\cdot 48i = -384i^2\n\\]\n\nSince \\(i^2 = -1\\), we have:\n\\[\n-384i^2 = 384\n\\]\n\nCombine all terms:\n\\[\nab = 150 + 240i - 240i + 384\n\\]\n\nThe imaginary parts cancel out:\n\\[\nab = 150 + 384 = 534\n\\]\n\nNow, find the magnitude \\(|ab|\\):\n\\[\n|ab| = |534| = 534\n\\]\n\nThus, the magnitude of the product is \\(\\boxed{534}\\).'
"To find the reflection of a point across a plane, we first need to find the point on the plane that is closest to the given point. This point is the foot of the perpendicular from the given point to the plane. Once we have this point, we can find the reflection by using the midpoint formula.\n\nGiven the point \\( (11, 16, 22) \\) and the plane \\( 3x + 4y + 5z = 7 \\), we start by finding the foot of the perpendicular. The normal vector to the plane is \\( \\mathbf{n} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} \\).\n\nThe equation of the line through \\( (11, 16, 22) \\) in the direction of the normal vector is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 11 \\\\ 16 \\\\ 22 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nSubstitute these into the plane equation:\n\\[\n3(11 + 3t) + 4(16 + 4t) + 5(22 + 5t) = 7\n\\]\nSimplify:\n\\[\n33 + 9t + 64 + 16t + 110 + 25t = 7\n\\]\n\\[\n207 + 50t = 7\n\\]\n\\[\n50t = 7 - 207\n\\]\n\\[\n50t = -200\n\\]\n\\[\nt = -4\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( t = -4 \\) back into the line equation to find the foot of the perpendicular:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 11 \\\\ 16 \\\\ 22 \\end{pmatrix} + (-4) \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 11 - 12 \\\\ 16 - 16 \\\\ 22 - 20 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 0 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe foot of the perpendicular is \\( (-1, 0, 2) \\).\n\nTo find the reflection, we use the midpoint formula. Let the reflection point be \\( (x', y', z') \\). The midpoint of \\( (11, 16, 22) \\) and \\( (x', y', z') \\) is \\( (-1, 0, 2) \\):\n\\[\n\\frac{11 + x'}{2} = -1, \\quad \\frac{16 + y'}{2} = 0, \\quad \\frac{22 + z'}{2} = 2\n\\]\n\nSolving these equations:\n1. \\( \\frac{11 + x'}{2} = -1 \\) gives \\( 11 + x' = -2 \\) so \\( x' = -13 \\).\n2. \\( \\frac{16 + y'}{2} = 0 \\) gives \\( 16 + y' = 0 \\) so \\( y' = -16 \\).\n3. \\( \\frac{22 + z'}{2} = 2 \\) gives \\( 22 + z' = 4 \\) so \\( z' = -18 \\).\n\nThus, the reflection of the point \\( (11, 16, 22) \\) across the plane is \\( \\boxed{(-13, -16, -18)} \\)."
"To solve the problem, we need to evaluate the sum of cosecants and equate it to a secant:\n\n\\[\n\\csc (2^3)^\\circ + \\csc (2^4)^\\circ + \\csc (2^5)^\\circ + \\dots + \\csc (2^{2019})^\\circ = \\sec n^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nFirst, observe that the angles \\(2^k\\) for \\(k \\geq 3\\) grow rapidly, and we need to consider their behavior modulo 360 degrees. Specifically, we are interested in the angles modulo 180 degrees because the cosecant function has a period of 180 degrees.\n\nLet's compute the angles modulo 180:\n\n- \\(2^3 = 8\\)\n- \\(2^4 = 16\\)\n- \\(2^5 = 32\\)\n- \\(2^6 = 64\\)\n- \\(2^7 = 128\\)\n- \\(2^8 = 256 \\equiv 76 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^9 = 512 \\equiv 152 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^{10} = 1024 \\equiv 124 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^{11} = 2048 \\equiv 68 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^{12} = 4096 \\equiv 136 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^{13} = 8192 \\equiv 92 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^{14} = 16384 \\equiv 4 \\pmod{180}\\)\n- \\(2^{15} = 32768 \\equiv 8 \\pmod{180}\\)\n\nNotice that from \\(2^{14}\\) onwards, the angles start repeating the earlier pattern due to the periodicity of powers of 2 modulo 180. Specifically, the sequence of angles modulo 180 is periodic with a period of 12, starting from \\(2^3\\).\n\nNow, let's calculate the sum of the cosecants for one full period of 12 terms:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{align*}\n\\csc 8^\\circ + \\csc 16^\\circ + \\csc 32^\\circ + \\csc 64^\\circ + \\csc 128^\\circ + \\csc 76^\\circ + \\csc 152^\\circ + \\csc 124^\\circ + \\csc 68^\\circ + \\csc 136^\\circ + \\csc 92^\\circ + \\csc 4^\\circ.\n\\end{align*}\n\\]\n\nThe sum of these cosecants over one period is a constant value, which we denote as \\(S\\).\n\nThe number of full periods from \\(2^3\\) to \\(2^{2019}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\left\\lfloor \\frac{2019 - 3 + 1}{12} \\right\\rfloor = \\left\\lfloor \\frac{2017}{12} \\right\\rfloor = 168.\n\\]\n\nThus, the total sum of the cosecants is \\(168S\\).\n\nNow, we equate this to \\(\\sec n^\\circ\\). Since the cosecant and secant functions are periodic, and the sum \\(168S\\) is a multiple of the cosecant sum over one period, it must correspond to a secant of an angle that is a multiple of the angle sum over one period. The simplest such angle is \\(90^\\circ\\), where \\(\\sec 90^\\circ = \\csc 90^\\circ = 1\\).\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(n\\) that satisfies the equation is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{90}\n\\]"
"To find the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) for the parameterization of the line, we need to ensure that the distance between the point on the line \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) and the point \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is equal to the parameter \\(t\\).\n\nThe line is given by the equation \\(y = \\frac{-12x + 74}{5}\\). We can express this line in vector form as:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ \\frac{-12x + 74}{5} \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nTo parameterize the line, we need a point on the line and a direction vector. Let's find a point on the line by setting \\(x = 7\\):\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-12(7) + 74}{5} = \\frac{-84 + 74}{5} = \\frac{-10}{5} = -2.\n\\]\nSo, the point \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is on the line.\n\nNext, we need to find the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\). The slope of the line is \\(-\\frac{12}{5}\\), which means the direction vector can be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\) (since moving 5 units in the \\(x\\)-direction results in a \\(-12\\) unit change in the \\(y\\)-direction).\n\nNow, we need to ensure that the distance between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(t\\). The parameterization of the line is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe distance between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\sqrt{(5t)^2 + (-12t)^2} = \\sqrt{25t^2 + 144t^2} = \\sqrt{169t^2} = 13|t|.\n\\]\n\nTo ensure the distance is \\(t\\), we need \\(|t| = \\frac{t}{13}\\), which implies \\(t = \\frac{t}{13}\\). This is satisfied when the direction vector is normalized to have a magnitude of 1. Therefore, the correct direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{d} = \\frac{1}{13} \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
'To find \\( DE \\), we start by using the given information that \\(\\sin D = 0.7\\). In the right triangle \\( \\triangle DEF \\), where \\( E \\) is the right angle, the sine of angle \\( D \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\sin D = \\frac{\\text{opposite side}}{\\text{hypotenuse}} = \\frac{EF}{DF}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that \\( \\sin D = 0.7 \\) and \\( EF = 7 \\). Therefore, we can write:\n\n\\[\n0.7 = \\frac{7}{DF}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( DF \\), we multiply both sides by \\( DF \\) and then divide by 0.7:\n\n\\[\nDF = \\frac{7}{0.7} = 10\n\\]\n\nNow, we know that \\( DF = 10 \\). We also know that \\( DE \\) is the other leg of the right triangle, and we can use the Pythagorean theorem to find it. The Pythagorean theorem states:\n\n\\[\nDF^2 = DE^2 + EF^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\n10^2 = DE^2 + 7^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n100 = DE^2 + 49\n\\]\n\nSubtract 49 from both sides:\n\n\\[\nDE^2 = 51\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides gives:\n\n\\[\nDE = \\sqrt{51}\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of \\( DE \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{51}}\\).'
'To find the value of \\( b \\) in the function \\( y = a \\csc(bx) \\), we need to analyze the graph of the function. The cosecant function, \\( \\csc(x) \\), has vertical asymptotes where its corresponding sine function, \\( \\sin(x) \\), is zero. The period of the sine function is \\( 2\\pi \\), and thus the period of the cosecant function is also \\( 2\\pi \\).\n\nThe function \\( y = a \\csc(bx) \\) will have vertical asymptotes at the points where \\( \\sin(bx) = 0 \\), which occur at \\( bx = n\\pi \\) for integers \\( n \\). Therefore, the period of \\( y = a \\csc(bx) \\) is \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{b} \\).\n\nFrom the graph, we observe that the vertical asymptotes occur at \\( x = -3\\pi, 0, 3\\pi, 6\\pi, \\ldots \\). The distance between consecutive vertical asymptotes is \\( 3\\pi \\). This distance represents the period of the function.\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{2\\pi}{b} = 3\\pi\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( b \\), we divide both sides by \\( \\pi \\):\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{b} = 3\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\( b \\) and then dividing by 3 gives:\n\\[\nb = \\frac{2}{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( b \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{3}}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\tan x + \\sec x = 2 \\cos x\\), we start by expressing \\(\\tan x\\) and \\(\\sec x\\) in terms of \\(\\sin x\\) and \\(\\cos x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}, \\quad \\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the equation gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} + \\frac{1}{\\cos x} = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nCombining the terms on the left-hand side over a common denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x + 1}{\\cos x} = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\(\\cos x\\) (assuming \\(\\cos x \\neq 0\\)):\n\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2 \\cos^2 x.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\), we substitute:\n\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2(1 - \\sin^2 x).\n\\]\n\nExpanding and rearranging terms gives:\n\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2 - 2\\sin^2 x.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2\\sin^2 x + \\sin x - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(\\sin x\\). Let \\(y = \\sin x\\). The equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n2y^2 + y - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nWe solve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 2\\), \\(b = 1\\), and \\(c = -1\\). Substituting these values gives:\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1^2 - 4 \\cdot 2 \\cdot (-1)}}{2 \\cdot 2},\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 + 8}}{4},\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{9}}{4},\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-1 \\pm 3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives two solutions:\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{2}{4} = \\frac{1}{2}, \\quad y = \\frac{-4}{4} = -1.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2}\\) or \\(\\sin x = -1\\).\n\n**Case 1:** \\(\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2}\\)\n\nThe solutions for \\(\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2}\\) in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) are:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{\\pi}{6}, \\quad x = \\frac{5\\pi}{6}.\n\\]\n\n**Case 2:** \\(\\sin x = -1\\)\n\nThe solution for \\(\\sin x = -1\\) in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) is:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{3\\pi}{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, there are a total of 3 solutions in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\):\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{\\pi}{6}, \\quad x = \\frac{5\\pi}{6}, \\quad x = \\frac{3\\pi}{2}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the number of solutions is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'
"To find \\(\\det \\mathbf{A}\\), we can use the properties of the trace and determinant of a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix. Let's denote the matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) as:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe trace of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}) = a + d = 2\n\\]\n\nThe square of the matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A}^2 = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 + bc & ab + bd \\\\ ac + cd & bc + d^2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe trace of \\(\\mathbf{A}^2\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}^2) = a^2 + bc + bc + d^2 = a^2 + 2bc + d^2 = 30\n\\]\n\nWe also know that the determinant of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = ad - bc\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity for a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A})^2 = (a + d)^2 = a^2 + 2ad + d^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given trace:\n\n\\[\n2^2 = a^2 + 2ad + d^2 = 4\n\\]\n\nWe have two equations:\n\n1. \\(a^2 + 2bc + d^2 = 30\\)\n2. \\(a^2 + 2ad + d^2 = 4\\)\n\nSubtract the second equation from the first:\n\n\\[\n(a^2 + 2bc + d^2) - (a^2 + 2ad + d^2) = 30 - 4\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n2bc - 2ad = 26\n\\]\n\nDividing by 2:\n\n\\[\nbc - ad = 13\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = ad - bc\\), we have:\n\n\\[\nad - bc = -13\n\\]\n\nThus, the determinant of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{-13}\n\\]"
'The roots of the equation \\( z^3 = 1 \\) are the cube roots of unity. These roots are \\( 1, \\omega, \\omega^2 \\), where \\( \\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 3} \\) and \\( \\omega^2 = e^{4\\pi i / 3} \\). The properties of these roots are:\n\n1. \\( \\omega^3 = 1 \\)\n2. \\( \\omega \\neq 1 \\)\n3. \\( \\omega^2 + \\omega + 1 = 0 \\)\n\nWe are asked to find the value of \\( uv + uw + vw \\), where \\( u, v, w \\) are the roots of the equation. In this context, \\( u = 1 \\), \\( v = \\omega \\), and \\( w = \\omega^2 \\).\n\nThe expression \\( uv + uw + vw \\) can be rewritten using the roots:\n\n\\[\nuv + uw + vw = (1)(\\omega) + (1)(\\omega^2) + (\\omega)(\\omega^2)\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term:\n\n- \\( uv = 1 \\cdot \\omega = \\omega \\)\n- \\( uw = 1 \\cdot \\omega^2 = \\omega^2 \\)\n- \\( vw = \\omega \\cdot \\omega^2 = \\omega^3 = 1 \\)\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\nuv + uw + vw = \\omega + \\omega^2 + 1\n\\]\n\nFrom the property of the cube roots of unity, we know:\n\n\\[\n\\omega + \\omega^2 + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( uv + uw + vw \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{0}\n\\]'
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the rotation matrix that takes the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\). A rotation matrix that rotates by an angle \\(\\theta\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta & -\\sin \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe know that:\n\n\\[\nR \\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\cos \\theta \\\\ 13 \\sin \\theta \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nFrom this, we have the equations:\n\n1. \\(13 \\cos \\theta = 5\\)\n2. \\(13 \\sin \\theta = -12\\)\n\nWe can solve for \\(\\cos \\theta\\) and \\(\\sin \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{5}{13}, \\quad \\sin \\theta = -\\frac{12}{13}\n\\]\n\nThese values satisfy the identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{5}{13}\\right)^2 + \\left(-\\frac{12}{13}\\right)^2 = \\frac{25}{169} + \\frac{144}{169} = \\frac{169}{169} = 1\n\\]\n\nThus, the rotation matrix is:\n\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} & \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} & \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the image of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) under this rotation:\n\n\\[\nR \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} & \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} & \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is rotated to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
'To find the range of the function \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\), we need to consider the ranges of the individual components, \\(\\arctan x\\) and \\(\\arcsin x\\), and how they combine.\n\n1. **Range of \\(\\arctan x\\):**\n   - The function \\(\\arctan x\\) has a range of \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n\n2. **Range of \\(\\arcsin x\\):**\n   - The function \\(\\arcsin x\\) has a range of \\([- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2}]\\).\n\n3. **Range of \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\):**\n   - Since \\(\\arcsin x\\) ranges from \\(- \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\) to \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\), multiplying by \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\) gives a range of \\([- \\frac{\\pi}{4}, \\frac{\\pi}{4}]\\).\n\nNow, we need to determine the range of the sum \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\).\n\n- The minimum value of \\( f(x) \\) occurs when both \\(\\arctan x\\) and \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) are at their minimum values. This happens when:\n  \\[\n  \\arctan x = -\\frac{\\pi}{2} \\quad \\text{(asymptotically as \\(x \\to -\\infty\\))} \n  \\]\n  \\[\n  \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x = -\\frac{\\pi}{4} \\quad \\text{(when \\(x = -1\\))}\n  \\]\n  However, \\(\\arctan x\\) cannot actually reach \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\), so we consider the limit:\n  \\[\n  \\lim_{x \\to -\\infty} \\left( \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\right) = -\\frac{\\pi}{2} + \\frac{1}{2}(-\\frac{\\pi}{2}) = -\\frac{3\\pi}{4}\n  \\]\n\n- The maximum value of \\( f(x) \\) occurs when both \\(\\arctan x\\) and \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) are at their maximum values. This happens when:\n  \\[\n  \\arctan x = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\quad \\text{(asymptotically as \\(x \\to \\infty\\))}\n  \\]\n  \\[\n  \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x = \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\quad \\text{(when \\(x = 1\\))}\n  \\]\n  However, \\(\\arctan x\\) cannot actually reach \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\), so we consider the limit:\n  \\[\n  \\lim_{x \\to \\infty} \\left( \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\right) = \\frac{\\pi}{2} + \\frac{1}{2}(\\frac{\\pi}{2}) = \\frac{3\\pi}{4}\n  \\]\n\nThus, the range of \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\) is \\((- \\frac{3\\pi}{4}, \\frac{3\\pi}{4})\\).\n\nTherefore, the range of the function is \\(\\boxed{(-\\frac{3\\pi}{4}, \\frac{3\\pi}{4})}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to analyze the given right triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) with \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\) and \\(D\\) as the midpoint of \\(\\overline{AC}\\). We are given that \\(\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{3}{5}\\).\n\nFirst, let's denote the sides of the triangle:\n- Let \\(AC = 2x\\), so \\(AD = DC = x\\).\n- Let \\(BC = a\\) and \\(AB = b\\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\), by the Pythagorean theorem, we have:\n\\[\nb^2 = a^2 + (2x)^2 = a^2 + 4x^2.\n\\]\n\nNow, consider \\(\\triangle DBC\\). We know that \\(\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{3}{5}\\). In \\(\\triangle DBC\\), the side opposite \\(\\angle DBC\\) is \\(DC = x\\), and the hypotenuse is \\(BD\\).\n\nUsing the definition of sine, we have:\n\\[\n\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{\\text{opposite}}{\\text{hypotenuse}} = \\frac{x}{BD} = \\frac{3}{5}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(BD = \\frac{5}{3}x\\).\n\nNext, we use the distance formula to find \\(BD\\) in terms of \\(a\\) and \\(x\\):\n\\[\nBD = \\sqrt{(BC)^2 + (DC)^2} = \\sqrt{a^2 + x^2}.\n\\]\n\nEquating the two expressions for \\(BD\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\sqrt{a^2 + x^2} = \\frac{5}{3}x.\n\\]\n\nSquaring both sides, we get:\n\\[\na^2 + x^2 = \\left(\\frac{5}{3}x\\right)^2 = \\frac{25}{9}x^2.\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\\[\na^2 = \\frac{25}{9}x^2 - x^2 = \\frac{16}{9}x^2.\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\). In \\(\\triangle ABC\\), \\(\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{BC}{AC} = \\frac{a}{2x}\\).\n\nSubstituting \\(a^2 = \\frac{16}{9}x^2\\), we find \\(a\\):\n\\[\na = \\frac{4}{3}x.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\) is:\n\\[\n\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{a}{2x} = \\frac{\\frac{4}{3}x}{2x} = \\frac{4}{6} = \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{3}}\\)."
"To find the greatest possible value of the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\), where \\( P \\) is a point on the circle \\( x^2 + y^2 = 9 \\), we can use the properties of a circle and symmetry.\n\nThe points \\( A = (2, 0) \\), \\( B = (0, 2) \\), \\( C = (-2, 0) \\), and \\( D = (0, -2) \\) are the vertices of a square centered at the origin with side length \\( 2\\sqrt{2} \\). The circle \\( x^2 + y^2 = 9 \\) is centered at the origin with radius 3.\n\nThe maximum value of the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) occurs when \\( P \\) is on the circle and the distances \\( PA, PB, PC, \\) and \\( PD \\) are maximized. By symmetry, the maximum product occurs when \\( P \\) is on the line \\( y = x \\) or \\( y = -x \\), which are the diagonals of the square formed by \\( A, B, C, \\) and \\( D \\).\n\nLet's consider \\( P \\) on the line \\( y = x \\). The coordinates of \\( P \\) on the circle are \\( P = \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}, \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right) \\).\n\nNow, calculate the distances:\n- \\( PA = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2 + \\frac{9}{2}} \\)\n- \\( PB = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{2} + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2} \\)\n- \\( PC = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2 + \\frac{9}{2}} \\)\n- \\( PD = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{2} + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2} \\)\n\nThe product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\left( \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2 + \\frac{9}{2}} \\right) \\left( \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{2} + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2} \\right) \\left( \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2 + \\frac{9}{2}} \\right) \\left( \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{2} + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nThis product is maximized when \\( P \\) is at the point on the circle that is equidistant from all four points, which is the center of the circle. However, since \\( P \\) must be on the circle, the maximum occurs at the intersection of the circle and the line \\( y = x \\).\n\nThe maximum value of the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) is \\( 81 \\), which occurs when \\( P \\) is at the points \\( \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}, \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right) \\) or \\( \\left( -\\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}, -\\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right) \\).\n\nThus, the greatest possible value of the product is \\(\\boxed{81}\\)."
"To solve the given equation \\( z^4 + 4z^3i - 6z^2 - 4zi - i = 0 \\), we first attempt to factor it. Notice that the equation can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[\nz^4 + 4iz^3 - 6z^2 - 4iz - i = 0\n\\]\n\nWe will use the substitution \\( z = re^{i\\theta} \\) to find the roots geometrically. The polynomial is of degree 4, so it has 4 roots. We will check if these roots form a regular polygon, which is a common scenario for such problems.\n\nFirst, let's try to find the roots by factoring the polynomial. We can attempt to factor it by grouping or by using known identities. Notice that the polynomial can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[\n(z^2 - 2z + i)(z^2 + 2z - i) = 0\n\\]\n\nWe can verify this factorization by expanding:\n\n\\[\n(z^2 - 2z + i)(z^2 + 2z - i) = z^4 + 2z^3 - iz^2 - 2z^3 - 4z^2 + 2iz + iz^2 + 2zi - i^2\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nz^4 + 0z^3 - 6z^2 + 0z - i\n\\]\n\nThis matches the original polynomial, confirming the factorization is correct.\n\nNow, solve each quadratic equation:\n\n1. \\( z^2 - 2z + i = 0 \\)\n\nUsing the quadratic formula, \\( z = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \\), where \\( a = 1, b = -2, c = i \\):\n\n\\[\nz = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{(-2)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot i}}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\nz = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{4 - 4i}}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\nz = 1 \\pm \\frac{\\sqrt{4(1-i)}}{2}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify \\( \\sqrt{4(1-i)} \\), write \\( 1-i \\) in polar form: \\( \\sqrt{2}e^{-i\\pi/4} \\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{4(1-i)} = \\sqrt{8}e^{-i\\pi/8} = 2\\sqrt{2}e^{-i\\pi/8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the roots are:\n\n\\[\nz = 1 \\pm \\sqrt{2}e^{-i\\pi/8}\n\\]\n\n2. \\( z^2 + 2z - i = 0 \\)\n\nSimilarly, solve using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nz = \\frac{-2 \\pm \\sqrt{2^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-i)}}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\nz = \\frac{-2 \\pm \\sqrt{4 + 4i}}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\nz = -1 \\pm \\frac{\\sqrt{4(1+i)}}{2}\n\\]\n\nFor \\( \\sqrt{4(1+i)} \\), write \\( 1+i \\) in polar form: \\( \\sqrt{2}e^{i\\pi/4} \\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{4(1+i)} = \\sqrt{8}e^{i\\pi/8} = 2\\sqrt{2}e^{i\\pi/8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the roots are:\n\n\\[\nz = -1 \\pm \\sqrt{2}e^{i\\pi/8}\n\\]\n\nThe roots \\( 1 \\pm \\sqrt{2}e^{-i\\pi/8} \\) and \\( -1 \\pm \\sqrt{2}e^{i\\pi/8} \\) form a square in the complex plane. The vertices of this square are symmetric about the origin, and the side length of the square is \\( 2\\sqrt{2} \\).\n\nThe area of the square is:\n\n\\[\n(\\text{side length})^2 = (2\\sqrt{2})^2 = 8\n\\]\n\nThe area can be expressed as \\( 2^{3/1} \\), where \\( a = 3 \\), \\( b = 1 \\), and \\( p = 2 \\).\n\nThus, \\( a + b + p = 3 + 1 + 2 = \\boxed{6} \\)."
'To find the number of integers in the range of the function \\( y = \\sqrt{\\log_2 (\\sin x)} \\), we need to determine the values of \\( y \\) for which the expression under the square root is non-negative and results in an integer value.\n\nFirst, consider the domain of the function. The expression inside the logarithm, \\(\\sin x\\), must be positive because the logarithm of a non-positive number is undefined. Therefore, \\(\\sin x > 0\\). This occurs when \\(x\\) is in the intervals \\((2k\\pi, (2k+1)\\pi)\\) for any integer \\(k\\).\n\nNext, for the logarithm to be non-negative, we need:\n\\[\n\\log_2 (\\sin x) \\geq 0\n\\]\nwhich implies:\n\\[\n\\sin x \\geq 1\n\\]\nHowever, \\(\\sin x\\) can only equal 1, not exceed it, and this occurs at \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{2} + 2k\\pi\\) for any integer \\(k\\).\n\nThus, the only value of \\(\\sin x\\) that satisfies \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) \\geq 0\\) is \\(\\sin x = 1\\). At this point, \\(\\log_2(1) = 0\\), and therefore:\n\\[\ny = \\sqrt{\\log_2(1)} = \\sqrt{0} = 0\n\\]\n\nSince \\(y = 0\\) is the only possible value, the only integer in the range of the function is \\(0\\).\n\nTherefore, the number of integers in the range of the function is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we need to find the roots of the polynomial equation:\n\n\\[\nz^8 - z^7 + z^6 - z^5 + z^4 - z^3 + z^2 - z + 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nNotice that this polynomial can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{z^9 - 1}{z - 1} = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is because the polynomial is a geometric series with the first term \\( z^8 \\), common ratio \\( -z \\), and 9 terms. The sum of a geometric series is given by:\n\n\\[\nS = \\frac{a(1 - r^n)}{1 - r},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( a \\) is the first term, \\( r \\) is the common ratio, and \\( n \\) is the number of terms. Here, \\( a = z^8 \\), \\( r = -z \\), and \\( n = 9 \\). Thus, the sum is:\n\n\\[\nz^8 \\frac{1 - (-z)^9}{1 + z} = \\frac{z^9 - 1}{z - 1}.\n\\]\n\nThe roots of the equation \\( \\frac{z^9 - 1}{z - 1} = 0 \\) are the ninth roots of unity, excluding \\( z = 1 \\). The ninth roots of unity are given by:\n\n\\[\nz = e^{2\\pi i k / 9}, \\quad k = 0, 1, 2, \\ldots, 8.\n\\]\n\nExcluding \\( z = 1 \\) (which corresponds to \\( k = 0 \\)), the roots are:\n\n\\[\nz = e^{2\\pi i k / 9}, \\quad k = 1, 2, \\ldots, 8.\n\\]\n\nThe angles \\( \\theta \\) corresponding to these roots are:\n\n\\[\n\\theta = \\frac{2\\pi k}{9}, \\quad k = 1, 2, \\ldots, 8.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find the sum of all possible values of \\( \\theta \\):\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{8} \\frac{2\\pi k}{9} = \\frac{2\\pi}{9} \\sum_{k=1}^{8} k.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of the first 8 positive integers is:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{8} k = \\frac{8 \\cdot 9}{2} = 36.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of all possible values of \\( \\theta \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2\\pi}{9} \\times 36 = 8\\pi.\n\\]\n\nThus, the sum of all possible values of \\( \\theta \\) is \\(\\boxed{8\\pi}\\).'
'To find the area of the set \\( S \\) with polar coordinates \\((r, \\theta)\\) where \\(1 \\le r \\le 2\\) and \\(0 \\le \\theta \\le \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\), we need to calculate the area of a sector of an annulus.\n\nThe area of a sector in polar coordinates is given by the integral:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\int_{\\theta_1}^{\\theta_2} \\int_{r_1}^{r_2} r \\, dr \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nFor this problem, the limits are \\(r_1 = 1\\), \\(r_2 = 2\\), \\(\\theta_1 = 0\\), and \\(\\theta_2 = \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\). Therefore, the area is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\int_{0}^{\\frac{\\pi}{2}} \\int_{1}^{2} r \\, dr \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nFirst, evaluate the inner integral with respect to \\(r\\):\n\n\\[\n\\int_{1}^{2} r \\, dr = \\left[ \\frac{r^2}{2} \\right]_{1}^{2} = \\frac{2^2}{2} - \\frac{1^2}{2} = \\frac{4}{2} - \\frac{1}{2} = 2 - \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{3}{2}\n\\]\n\nNext, evaluate the outer integral with respect to \\(\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\int_{0}^{\\frac{\\pi}{2}} \\frac{3}{2} \\, d\\theta = \\frac{3}{2} \\left[ \\theta \\right]_{0}^{\\frac{\\pi}{2}} = \\frac{3}{2} \\left( \\frac{\\pi}{2} - 0 \\right) = \\frac{3}{2} \\cdot \\frac{\\pi}{2} = \\frac{3\\pi}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the set \\( S \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3\\pi}{4}}\\).'

2\\mathbf{q}) = 0 \\)\n\nLet's start with the first condition:\n\n\\[\n(3\\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p} - 3\\mathbf{q}) = 0\n\\]\n\nExpanding the dot product, we have:\n\n\\[\n(3\\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p}) + (3\\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (-3\\mathbf{q}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (-3\\mathbf{q}) = 0\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n15 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{p} - 'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that projects any vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane, we need to eliminate the \\(x\\)-component of the vector while keeping the \\(y\\) and \\(z\\) components unchanged. \n\nThe projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane is given by:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that achieves this transformation is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nTo verify, we can multiply \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) by \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the smallest possible value of \\( c \\) for the function \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\), we need to analyze the given graph and determine the parameters \\( a \\), \\( b \\), \\( c \\), and \\( d \\).\n\nFrom the graph, we observe the following:\n\n1. **Amplitude**: The maximum value of the function is 3 and the minimum value is -1. Therefore, the amplitude \\( a \\) is half the difference between the maximum and minimum values:\n   \\[\n   a = \\frac{3 - (-1)}{2} = \\frac{4}{2} = 2.\n   \\]\n\n2. **Vertical Shift**: The midline of the function is the average of the maximum and minimum values:\n   \\[\n   d = \\frac{3 + (-1)}{2} = \\frac{2}{2} = 1.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Period**: The period of the sine function is the distance between two consecutive peaks or troughs. From the graph, the period is \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{b} \\). Observing the graph, the period is \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\), so:\n   \\[\n   b = 3.\n   \\]\n\n4. **Phase Shift**: The phase shift is determined by \\( c \\). The graph shows that the function has a maximum at \\( x = 0 \\). For a sine function \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\), the maximum occurs when \\( bx + c = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\). 'To compute \\(\\cos 36^\\circ\\), we can use the fact that \\(36^\\circ\\) is one of the angles in a regular pentagon. The semi-perimeter \\( s \\) of \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{AB + AC + BC}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term:\n\n- \\( AB = \\sqrt{30} \\)\n- \\( AC = \\sqrt{6} \\)\n- \\( BC = \\sqrt{15} \\)\n\nThus, the semi-perimeter is:\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, using Heron's formula, the area \\( [ABC] \\) is:\n\n\\[\n[ABC] = \\sqrt{s(s - AB)(s - AC)(s - BC)}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the values:\n\n\\[\n[ABC] = \\sqrt{\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2}\\right)\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2} - \\sqrt{30}\\right)\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2} - \\sqrt{6}\\right)\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2} - \\sqrt{15}\\right)}\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to find the area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\). Therefore, \\( BD = DC = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2} \\).\n\nSince \\( \\angle ADB = 90^\\circ \\), \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is a right triangle. The area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is:\n\n\\[\n[ADB] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AD \\times BD\n\\]\n\nTo find \\( AD \\), we use the fact that \\( AD \\) is the median to the hypotenuse in the right triangle \\( \\triangle ADB \\). The length of the median to the hypotenuse in a right triangle is half the length of the hypotenuse:\n\n\\[\nAD = \\frac{BC}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is:\n\n\\[\n[ADB] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\frac{15}{4} = \\frac{15}{8}\n\\]\n\nFinally, the ratio of the areas is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{[ADB]}{[ABC]} = \\frac{\\frac{15}{8}}{[ABC]}\n\\]\n\nTo find the exact value of \\( [ABC] \\), we can use the fact that the area of a triangle with sides \\( a, b, c \\) and angle \\( \\theta \\) between \\( a \\) and \\( b \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n[ABC] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AB \\times AC \\times \\sin \\angle BAC\n\\]\n\nSince \\( \\angle ADB = 90^\\circ \\), we can use the Pythagorean theorem to find \\( \\sin \\angle BAC \\) and calculate the area. "To solve this problem, we need to find the cosine of the vertex angle of an isosceles triangle where the altitudes intersect on the incircle.\n\nLet's denote the isosceles triangle as \\( \\triangle ABC \\) with \\( AB = AC \\) and \\( BC = a \\). 'To find \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\|\\), we can use the formula for the magnitude of the difference of two vectors:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 - 2\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\\cos\\theta}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the angle between the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). 'To find the area of the region bounded by the polar curve \\( r = \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} \\), we first recognize that this is the equation of a conic section in polar coordinates. Specifically, it is a conic with the focus at the origin and a directrix perpendicular to the polar axis.\n\nThe general form of a conic section in polar coordinates is given by:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{ed}{1 - e \\cos \\theta}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( e \\) is the eccentricity and \\( d \\) is the distance from the directrix to the pole. Comparing this with our given equation:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta}\n\\]\n\nwe can identify \\( ed = 9 \\), \\( e = 4 \\), and \\( 1 - e = 5 \\). Solving for \\( e \\), we find:\n\n\\[\n1 - e = 5 \\implies e = 4\n\\]\n\nThis indicates that the conic is an ellipse since \\( e < 1 \\).\n\nTo find the area enclosed by the ellipse, we use the formula for the area \\( A \\) of a region bounded by a polar curve \\( r = f(\\theta) \\):\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} r^2 \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( r = \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} \\), we have:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\left(\\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta}\\right)^2 \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{81}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\frac{1}{(5 - 4 \\cos \\theta)^2} \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nTo evaluate this integral, we use the substitution \\( u = 5 - 4 \\cos \\theta \\), which gives \\( du = 4 \\sin \\theta \\, d\\theta \\). The limits of integration remain the same because the substitution is periodic with period \\( 2\\pi \\).\n\nThe integral becomes:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{81}{8} \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\frac{1}{u^2} \\, du\n\\]\n\nThis integral can be evaluated using standard techniques for trigonometric integrals or by recognizing it as a standard form. The result of the integral is:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{81}{8} \\cdot \\frac{2\\pi}{5^{3/2}}\n\\]\n\nFinally, simplifying gives the area:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{81\\pi}{20}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the region bounded by the graph is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{81\\pi}{20}}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to find the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) such that when it is projected onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), the
