'To find the inverse of the product \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\), we use the property that the inverse of a product of matrices is the product of their inverses in reverse order. Specifically, if \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{B}\\) are invertible matrices, then:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B})^{-1} = \\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\n\\]\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 0 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{B}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 \\\\ -1 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe need to compute \\(\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 \\\\ -1 & 3 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 0 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo perform the matrix multiplication, we calculate each element of the resulting matrix:\n\n- The element in the first row, first column:\n  \\[\n  (2 \\cdot 4) + (-1 \\cdot 1) = 8 - 1 = 7\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the first row, second column:\n  \\[\n  (2 \\cdot 0) + (-1 \\cdot -1) = 0 + 1 = 1\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the second row, first column:\n  \\[\n  (-1 \\cdot 4) + (3 \\cdot 1) = -4 + 3 = -1\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the second row, second column:\n  \\[\n  (-1 \\cdot 0) + (3 \\cdot -1) = 0 - 3 = -3\n  \\]\n\nThus, the inverse of \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B})^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the inverse of \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are collinear and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), we can use the property of angle bisectors in vector spaces.\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}, \\quad \\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) can be expressed as a scalar multiple of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) such that the angle bisector condition is satisfied. The angle bisector condition in vector terms is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|} + \\frac{\\mathbf{c}}{\\|\\mathbf{c}\\|}\n\\]\n\nHowever, since \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is the angle bisector, we can use the property that the angle bisector of two vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is proportional to the sum of the unit vectors in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\). Therefore, we can write:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} = k(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{c})\n\\]\n\nfor some scalar \\(k\\). Solving for \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{c} = \\frac{1}{k}\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(k\\), we use the fact that \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is the angle bisector, which implies:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\|\\mathbf{c}\\|\n\\]\n\nCalculating the magnitudes:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{7^2 + (-1)^2 + 4^2} = \\sqrt{66}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{3^2 + 1^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{14}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\|\\mathbf{c}\\|\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{14} = \\sqrt{66}\n\\]\n\nThis is not possible, indicating a mistake in the assumption. Instead, we should find \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is the angle bisector, which means:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{c} = \\lambda \\mathbf{a}\n\\]\n\nfor some scalar \\(\\lambda\\). Since \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle, the direction of \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is opposite to \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is the average direction:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{c}}{2}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\mathbf{c}\\):\n\n\\[\n2\\mathbf{b} = \\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{c}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{c} = 2\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the given vectors:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{c} = 2\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\\\ 2 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^3\\) and equate it to the given matrix.\n\nFirst, compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^2\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^2 = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ab + bd \\\\ 0 & d^2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nNext, compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^3\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\mathbf{M}^2 \\cdot \\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ab + bd \\\\ 0 & d^2 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^3 & a^2b + abd + b d^2 \\\\ 0 & d^3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe equate this to the given matrix:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a^3 & a^2b + abd + b d^2 \\\\ 0 & d^3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nFrom the equations, we have:\n1. \\(a^3 = 8\\),\n2. \\(d^3 = 27\\),\n3. \\(a^2b + abd + b d^2 = -57\\).\n\nSolving the first two equations:\n- \\(a^3 = 8\\) implies \\(a = 2\\) (since \\(2^3 = 8\\)),\n- \\(d^3 = 27\\) implies \\(d = 3\\) (since \\(3^3 = 27\\)).\n\nSubstitute \\(a = 2\\) and \\(d = 3\\) into the third equation:\n\\[\na^2b + abd + b d^2 = 4b + 6b + 9b = 19b = -57.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(b\\):\n\\[\n19b = -57 \\implies b = -3.\n\\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -3 \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) that satisfies \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -3 \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to analyze the given conditions in triangle \\(ABC\\) with a right angle at \\(C\\) and \\(CB > CA\\). We are given that point \\(D\\) is on \\(\\overline{BC}\\) such that \\(\\angle CAD = 2 \\angle DAB\\), and \\(\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{2}{3}\\). We need to find \\(\\frac{CD}{BD}\\).\n\nFirst, let's denote the angles:\n- Let \\(\\angle DAB = \\theta\\).\n- Then \\(\\angle CAD = 2\\theta\\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle CAB = \\angle CAD + \\angle DAB = 2\\theta + \\theta = 3\\theta\\), we have \\(\\angle CAB = 3\\theta\\).\n\nUsing the angle bisector theorem in \\(\\triangle CAD\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{CD}{BD}.\n\\]\nGiven \\(\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{2}{3}\\), it follows that:\n\\[\n\\frac{CD}{BD} = \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio \\(\\frac{CD}{BD}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{3}}\\)."
'To evaluate the expression \\(\\sin (\\arcsin 0.4 + \\arcsin 0.5) \\cdot \\sin (\\arcsin 0.5 - \\arcsin 0.4)\\), we start by letting \\(\\theta = \\arcsin 0.4\\) and \\(\\phi = \\arcsin 0.5\\). This implies that \\(\\sin \\theta = 0.4\\) and \\(\\sin \\phi = 0.5\\).\n\nWe need to find \\(\\sin(\\theta + \\phi)\\) and \\(\\sin(\\phi - \\theta)\\).\n\n### Step 1: Calculate \\(\\cos \\theta\\) and \\(\\cos \\phi\\)\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we find:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 \\theta} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.4^2} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.16} = \\sqrt{0.84} = \\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{5}\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for \\(\\phi\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\phi = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 \\phi} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.5^2} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.25} = \\sqrt{0.75} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Calculate \\(\\sin(\\theta + \\phi)\\)\n\nUsing the sine addition formula \\(\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) = \\sin \\theta \\cos \\phi + \\cos \\theta \\sin \\phi\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) = 0.4 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{5} \\cdot 0.5\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2} + \\frac{0.5\\sqrt{21}}{5}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{10}\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Calculate \\(\\sin(\\phi - \\theta)\\)\n\nUsing the sine subtraction formula \\(\\sin(\\phi - \\theta) = \\sin \\phi \\cos \\theta - \\cos \\phi \\sin \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(\\phi - \\theta) = 0.5 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{5} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\cdot 0.4\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{0.5\\sqrt{21}}{5} - \\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{10} - \\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\n### Step 4: Evaluate the product\n\nThe expression we need to evaluate is:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) \\cdot \\sin(\\phi - \\theta)\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the expressions we found:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{10}\\right) \\cdot \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{10} - \\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nThis is a difference of squares:\n\n\\[\n= \\left(\\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^2 - \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{10}\\right)^2\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{0.4\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^2 = \\frac{0.16 \\cdot 3}{4} = \\frac{0.48}{4} = 0.12\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{21}}{10}\\right)^2 = \\frac{21}{100} = 0.21\n\\]\n\nThus, the product is:\n\n\\[\n0.12 - 0.21 = -0.09\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{-0.09}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\), we can use the angle subtraction identity for cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a - b) = \\cos a \\cos b + \\sin a \\sin b\n\\]\n\nFor \\(a = 45^\\circ\\) and \\(b = 30^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\cos(45^\\circ - 30^\\circ) = \\cos 45^\\circ \\cos 30^\\circ + \\sin 45^\\circ \\sin 30^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe know the exact values:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}, \\quad \\cos 30^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}, \\quad \\sin 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}, \\quad \\sin 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these values into the identity, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right) + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)\\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{4} + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nCombine the fractions:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{2}}{4}}\\).'
"To find the value of \\( k \\) such that the given equation has infinitely many solutions, we need to ensure that the two lines represented by the parametric equations are the same line. This means that the direction vectors must be scalar multiples of each other, and the points on the lines must be consistent.\n\nThe given equation is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix} + s \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nFirst, let's check if the direction vectors are scalar multiples of each other:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe see that:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix} = -3 \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the direction vectors are indeed scalar multiples of each other.\n\nNext, we need to ensure that the points \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix}\\) are consistent with this scalar relationship. This means that there should exist a scalar \\( c \\) such that:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} + c \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( c \\), we equate the components:\n\n1. \\( 8 = 4 + 5c \\)\n2. \\( k = -1 + 2c \\)\n\nFrom the first equation:\n\n\\[\n8 = 4 + 5c \\implies 4 = 5c \\implies c = \\frac{4}{5}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( c = \\frac{4}{5} \\) into the second equation:\n\n\\[\nk = -1 + 2 \\left(\\frac{4}{5}\\right) = -1 + \\frac{8}{5} = -\\frac{5}{5} + \\frac{8}{5} = \\frac{3}{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( k \\) that makes the lines identical is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{5}}\\)."
'To find the period of the function \\( y = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\), we need to determine the smallest positive period \\( T \\) such that:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(x + T) = \\cos x, \\quad \\cos(2x + 2T) = \\cos 2x, \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\cos(3x + 3T) = \\cos 3x\n\\]\n\nThe period of \\( \\cos x \\) is \\( 2\\pi \\), the period of \\( \\cos 2x \\) is \\( \\pi \\), and the period of \\( \\cos 3x \\) is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThe period \\( T \\) of the entire function will be the least common multiple (LCM) of these individual periods:\n\n- The period of \\( \\cos x \\) is \\( 2\\pi \\).\n- The period of \\( \\cos 2x \\) is \\( \\pi \\).\n- The period of \\( \\cos 3x \\) is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nTo find the LCM, we first express these periods with a common denominator:\n\n- \\( 2\\pi = \\frac{6\\pi}{3} \\)\n- \\( \\pi = \\frac{3\\pi}{3} \\)\n- \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\)\n\nThe LCM of the numerators \\( 6, 3, \\) and \\( 2 \\) is \\( 6 \\). Therefore, the LCM of the periods is \\(\\frac{6\\pi}{3} = 2\\pi\\).\n\nThus, the period of the function \\( y = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\) is \\(\\boxed{2\\pi}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to find the two least positive real values of \\( x \\) for which the sine of \\( x \\) degrees is the same as the sine of \\( x \\) radians. This means we need to solve the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(x^\\circ) = \\sin(x \\text{ radians})\n\\]\n\nFirst, recall the conversion between degrees and radians: \\( x^\\circ = \\frac{\\pi}{180}x \\) radians. Therefore, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sin\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{180}x\\right) = \\sin(x)\n\\]\n\nThe sine function is periodic with period \\( 2\\pi \\), so the solutions to the equation \\(\\sin A = \\sin B\\) are given by:\n\n\\[\nA = B + 2k\\pi \\quad \\text{or} \\quad A = \\pi - B + 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\nfor any integer \\( k \\).\n\n### Case 1: \\( \\frac{\\pi}{180}x = x + 2k\\pi \\)\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi}{180}x - x = 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{180} - 1\\right) = 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2k\\pi}{1 - \\frac{\\pi}{180}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2k\\pi \\cdot 180}{180 - \\pi}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{360k\\pi}{180 - \\pi}\n\\]\n\n### Case 2: \\( \\frac{\\pi}{180}x = \\pi - x + 2k\\pi \\)\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi}{180}x + x = \\pi + 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx\\left(1 + \\frac{\\pi}{180}\\right) = \\pi(1 + 2k)\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{\\pi(1 + 2k)}{1 + \\frac{\\pi}{180}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{\\pi(1 + 2k) \\cdot 180}{180 + \\pi}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{180\\pi(1 + 2k)}{180 + \\pi}\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the two smallest positive values of \\( x \\).\n\n### Smallest Positive Values\n\n1. **From Case 1:**\n\n   For \\( k = 1 \\):\n\n   \\[\n   x = \\frac{360\\pi}{180 - \\pi}\n   \\]\n\n2. **From Case 2:**\n\n   For \\( k = 0 \\):\n\n   \\[\n   x = \\frac{180\\pi}{180 + \\pi}\n   \\]\n\nThese are the two smallest positive values of \\( x \\).\n\n### Final Calculation\n\nThe two values are:\n\n- \\(\\frac{360\\pi}{180 - \\pi}\\) corresponds to \\(\\frac{m\\pi}{n-\\pi}\\) with \\( m = 360 \\) and \\( n = 180 \\).\n- \\(\\frac{180\\pi}{180 + \\pi}\\) corresponds to \\(\\frac{p\\pi}{q+\\pi}\\) with \\( p = 180 \\) and \\( q = 180 \\).\n\nThus, \\( m+n+p+q = 360 + 180 + 180 + 180 = 900 \\).\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{900}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to use the given orthogonality conditions to find the relationship between the magnitudes of vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n1. **Orthogonality Condition 1: \\(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) are orthogonal.**\n\n   This means:\n   \\[\n   (\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}) \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0\n   \\]\n   Expanding the dot product, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = -\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\n   \\]\n\n2. **Orthogonality Condition 2: \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) are orthogonal.**\n\n   This means:\n   \\[\n   (\\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b}) \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 0\n   \\]\n   Expanding the dot product, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}) = 0\n   \\]\n   Substituting \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = -\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\\) from the first condition:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + 2(-\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2) = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 - 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 = 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\n   \\]\n\n3. **Finding \\(\\frac{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\\):**\n\n   From \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 = 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\\), we take the square root of both sides:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{2}\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\n   \\]\n   Therefore, the ratio of the magnitudes is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
'The determinant of the product of two matrices is the product of their determinants. Therefore, if \\(\\det \\mathbf{A} = -7\\) and \\(\\det \\mathbf{B} = 3\\), then the determinant of the product \\(\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}) = \\det (\\mathbf{B}) \\cdot \\det (\\mathbf{A}) = 3 \\cdot (-7) = -21.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}) = \\boxed{-21}.\\)'
"To solve the equation \\(\\sin 6x + \\cos 4x = 0\\), we start by using trigonometric identities to express the equation in a more manageable form. We can use the identity \\(\\sin A = \\cos(90^\\circ - A)\\) to rewrite the equation as:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(90^\\circ - 6x) + \\cos 4x = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(90^\\circ - 6x) = -\\cos 4x.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\cos A = -\\cos B\\) implies that \\(A = 180^\\circ - B + k \\cdot 360^\\circ\\) or \\(A = B + (2k+1) \\cdot 180^\\circ\\) for some integer \\(k\\). Applying this to our equation, we have:\n\n1. \\(90^\\circ - 6x = 180^\\circ - 4x + k \\cdot 360^\\circ\\)\n2. \\(90^\\circ - 6x = 4x + (2k+1) \\cdot 180^\\circ\\)\n\nLet's solve each case separately.\n\n**Case 1:**\n\n\\[\n90^\\circ - 6x = 180^\\circ - 4x + k \\cdot 360^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n-6x + 4x = 180^\\circ - 90^\\circ + k \\cdot 360^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-2x = 90^\\circ + k \\cdot 360^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = -45^\\circ - k \\cdot 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\), we need to find suitable \\(k\\):\n\n- For \\(k = -1\\), \\(x = -45^\\circ + 180^\\circ = 135^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, \\(x = 135^\\circ\\) is a solution from this case.\n\n**Case 2:**\n\n\\[\n90^\\circ - 6x = 4x + (2k+1) \\cdot 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n90^\\circ = 10x + (2k+1) \\cdot 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n10x = 90^\\circ - (2k+1) \\cdot 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{90^\\circ - (2k+1) \\cdot 180^\\circ}{10}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find suitable \\(k\\) such that \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\):\n\n- For \\(k = 0\\), \\(x = \\frac{90^\\circ - 180^\\circ}{10} = -9^\\circ\\) (not valid).\n- For \\(k = -1\\), \\(x = \\frac{90^\\circ + 180^\\circ}{10} = 27^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, \\(x = 27^\\circ\\) is a solution from this case.\n\nCombining the solutions from both cases, the angles \\(x\\) that satisfy the equation are:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{27^\\circ, 135^\\circ}\n\\]"
'To find the speed of the particle, we need to determine the magnitude of its velocity vector. The position of the particle at time \\( t \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{r}(t) = \\left( 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4}, 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right).\n\\]\n\nThe velocity vector \\(\\mathbf{v}(t)\\) is the derivative of the position vector \\(\\mathbf{r}(t)\\) with respect to time \\( t \\). We differentiate each component of \\(\\mathbf{r}(t)\\):\n\n1. Differentiate the \\( x \\)-component:\n   \\[\n   x(t) = 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\frac{dx}{dt} = 3 \\cdot \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\cdot \\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4}.\n   \\]\n\n2. Differentiate the \\( y \\)-component:\n   \\[\n   y(t) = 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\frac{dy}{dt} = -3 \\cdot \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\cdot \\frac{1}{4} = -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4}.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the velocity vector is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v}(t) = \\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4}, -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right).\n\\]\n\nThe speed of the particle is the magnitude of the velocity vector:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Speed} = \\left\\| \\mathbf{v}(t) \\right\\| = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right)^2 + \\left( -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right)^2}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression under the square root:\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\cos^2 \\frac{t}{4} + \\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\sin^2 \\frac{t}{4}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\left( \\cos^2 \\frac{t}{4} + \\sin^2 \\frac{t}{4} \\right)}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\cdot 1}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the speed of the particle is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{4}}\\) units of distance per unit of time.'
'To simplify the expression \\(4 \\sin x \\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x)\\), we can use trigonometric identities.\n\nFirst, we use the identity for the product of sines:\n\\[\n\\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(60^\\circ - x - (60^\\circ + x)) - \\cos(60^\\circ - x + 60^\\circ + x)].\n\\]\nSimplifying the arguments of the cosines, we have:\n\\[\n\\cos(60^\\circ - x - 60^\\circ - x) = \\cos(-2x) = \\cos(2x),\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos(60^\\circ - x + 60^\\circ + x) = \\cos(120^\\circ).\n\\]\nSince \\(\\cos(120^\\circ) = -\\frac{1}{2}\\), the identity becomes:\n\\[\n\\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) - (-\\frac{1}{2})] = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}].\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this back into the original expression, we have:\n\\[\n4 \\sin x \\left(\\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}]\\right) = 2 \\sin x [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}].\n\\]\n\nDistributing \\(2 \\sin x\\), we get:\n\\[\n2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) + \\sin x.\n\\]\n\nNow, using the identity \\(2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) = \\sin(3x) - \\sin x\\), we can simplify further:\n\\[\n2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) = \\sin(3x) - \\sin x.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\sin(3x) - \\sin x + \\sin x = \\sin(3x).\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{\\sin(3x)}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\( z^6 + z^3 + 1 = 0 \\), we first make a substitution. Let \\( w = z^3 \\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\nw^2 + w + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\( w \\). We can solve it using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 1 \\), and \\( c = 1 \\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot 1}}{2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 - 4}}{2} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{-3}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-1 \\pm i\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions for \\( w \\) are:\n\n\\[\nw_1 = \\frac{-1 + i\\sqrt{3}}{2}, \\quad w_2 = \\frac{-1 - i\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, recall that \\( w = z^3 \\). We need to find the cube roots of \\( w_1 \\) and \\( w_2 \\).\n\nFirst, consider \\( w_1 = \\frac{-1 + i\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\). This is a complex number in polar form. The modulus is:\n\n\\[\n|w_1| = \\sqrt{\\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{4} + \\frac{3}{4}} = \\sqrt{1} = 1\n\\]\n\nThe argument of \\( w_1 \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\arg(w_1) = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}/2}{-1/2}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(-\\sqrt{3}) = 120^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, in polar form, \\( w_1 = \\text{cis}(120^\\circ) \\).\n\nThe cube roots of \\( w_1 \\) are given by:\n\n\\[\nz = \\text{cis}\\left(\\frac{120^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{3}\\right) \\quad \\text{for } k = 0, 1, 2\n\\]\n\nCalculating these, we get:\n\n- For \\( k = 0 \\), \\( z = \\text{cis}(40^\\circ) \\)\n- For \\( k = 1 \\), \\( z = \\text{cis}(160^\\circ) \\)\n- For \\( k = 2 \\), \\( z = \\text{cis}(280^\\circ) \\)\n\nNext, consider \\( w_2 = \\frac{-1 - i\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\). The modulus is the same, \\( |w_2| = 1 \\), and the argument is:\n\n\\[\n\\arg(w_2) = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-\\sqrt{3}/2}{-1/2}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(\\sqrt{3}) = -120^\\circ \\equiv 240^\\circ \\pmod{360^\\circ}\n\\]\n\nThus, in polar form, \\( w_2 = \\text{cis}(240^\\circ) \\).\n\nThe cube roots of \\( w_2 \\) are given by:\n\n\\[\nz = \\text{cis}\\left(\\frac{240^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{3}\\right) \\quad \\text{for } k = 0, 1, 2\n\\]\n\nCalculating these, we get:\n\n- For \\( k = 0 \\), \\( z = \\text{cis}(80^\\circ) \\)\n- For \\( k = 1 \\), \\( z = \\text{cis}(200^\\circ) \\)\n- For \\( k = 2 \\), \\( z = \\text{cis}(320^\\circ) \\)\n\nAmong all these roots, the one with an argument between \\( 90^\\circ \\) and \\( 180^\\circ \\) is \\( z = \\text{cis}(160^\\circ) \\).\n\nTherefore, the argument \\(\\theta\\) of the complex root is \\(\\boxed{160^\\circ}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we start by using the identity for the sum of arccosines:\n\n\\[\n\\arccos x + \\arccos y + \\arccos z = \\pi \\quad \\text{implies} \\quad x^2 + y^2 + z^2 + 2xyz = 1\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\arccos x + \\arccos 2x + \\arccos 3x = \\pi\\), we set \\(x = x\\), \\(y = 2x\\), and \\(z = 3x\\). Applying the identity, we have:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + (2x)^2 + (3x)^2 + 2(x)(2x)(3x) = 1\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + 4x^2 + 9x^2 + 12x^3 = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n14x^2 + 12x^3 = 1\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we obtain the cubic equation:\n\n\\[\n12x^3 + 14x^2 - 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThe polynomial is \\(12x^3 + 14x^2 + 0x - 1 = 0\\). The coefficients are \\(a = 12\\), \\(b = 14\\), \\(c = 0\\), and \\(d = -1\\).\n\nTo find the smallest possible value of \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\), we calculate:\n\n\\[\n|12| + |14| + |0| + |-1| = 12 + 14 + 0 + 1 = 27\n\\]\n\nThus, the smallest possible value of \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\) is \\(\\boxed{27}\\).'
"To find the smallest positive angle \\(\\theta\\) for which \\(\\sin 3\\theta = \\cos 7\\theta\\), we start by using the identity \\(\\cos x = \\sin(90^\\circ - x)\\). This gives us:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 3\\theta = \\sin(90^\\circ - 7\\theta)\n\\]\n\nFor two sine functions to be equal, their arguments must either be equal or differ by a multiple of \\(180^\\circ\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n1. \\(3\\theta = 90^\\circ - 7\\theta + 360^\\circ n\\)\n2. \\(3\\theta = 180^\\circ - (90^\\circ - 7\\theta) + 360^\\circ n\\)\n\nLet's solve each case separately.\n\n### Case 1:\n\\[\n3\\theta = 90^\\circ - 7\\theta + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\\[\n3\\theta + 7\\theta = 90^\\circ + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\\[\n10\\theta = 90^\\circ + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\\[\n\\theta = 9^\\circ + 36^\\circ n\n\\]\n\n### Case 2:\n\\[\n3\\theta = 180^\\circ - (90^\\circ - 7\\theta) + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\\[\n3\\theta = 180^\\circ - 90^\\circ + 7\\theta + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\\[\n3\\theta = 90^\\circ + 7\\theta + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\\[\n3\\theta - 7\\theta = 90^\\circ + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\\[\n-4\\theta = 90^\\circ + 360^\\circ n\n\\]\n\\[\n\\theta = -\\frac{90^\\circ + 360^\\circ n}{4}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\theta = -22.5^\\circ - 90^\\circ n\n\\]\n\nSince we are looking for the smallest positive \\(\\theta\\), we focus on the solutions from Case 1, as Case 2 yields negative angles for small \\(n\\).\n\nFrom Case 1, the smallest positive \\(\\theta\\) occurs when \\(n = 0\\):\n\\[\n\\theta = 9^\\circ + 36^\\circ \\times 0 = 9^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the smallest positive angle \\(\\theta\\) for which \\(\\sin 3\\theta = \\cos 7\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{9^\\circ}\\)."
'To simplify the expression \\(\\frac{1}{1 - \\tan^2 x} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\cot^2 x}\\), we start by using the trigonometric identities for \\(\\tan x\\) and \\(\\cot x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\cot x = \\frac{\\cos x}{\\sin x}.\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan^2 x = \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\cot^2 x = \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nNow, consider the first term:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 - \\tan^2 x} = \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x}} = \\frac{1}{\\frac{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x}} = \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for the second term:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 - \\cot^2 x} = \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}} = \\frac{1}{\\frac{\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}} = \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\(\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x = -(\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x)\\). Therefore, we can rewrite the second term as:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x} = -\\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nNow, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x} - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nSince both terms have the same denominator, we can combine them:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x} = 1.\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'To find \\(\\cos(a + b)\\), we use the cosine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\cos a \\cos b - \\sin a \\sin b\n\\]\n\nWe are given \\(\\cos a = \\frac{3}{5}\\) and \\(\\cos b = \\frac{5}{13}\\). We need to find \\(\\sin a\\) and \\(\\sin b\\).\n\nSince \\(a\\) is an acute angle, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin a = \\sqrt{1 - \\cos^2 a} = \\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{3}{5}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{1 - \\frac{9}{25}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{16}{25}} = \\frac{4}{5}\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, since \\(b\\) is an acute angle, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin b = \\sqrt{1 - \\cos^2 b} = \\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{5}{13}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{1 - \\frac{25}{169}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{144}{169}} = \\frac{12}{13}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these values into the cosine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\left(\\frac{3}{5}\\right)\\left(\\frac{5}{13}\\right) - \\left(\\frac{4}{5}\\right)\\left(\\frac{12}{13}\\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate each term:\n\n\\[\n\\cos a \\cos b = \\frac{3}{5} \\cdot \\frac{5}{13} = \\frac{15}{65} = \\frac{3}{13}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin a \\sin b = \\frac{4}{5} \\cdot \\frac{12}{13} = \\frac{48}{65}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back into the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\frac{3}{13} - \\frac{48}{65}\n\\]\n\nTo subtract these fractions, convert \\(\\frac{3}{13}\\) to a fraction with a denominator of 65:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{13} = \\frac{3 \\times 5}{13 \\times 5} = \\frac{15}{65}\n\\]\n\nNow perform the subtraction:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\frac{15}{65} - \\frac{48}{65} = \\frac{15 - 48}{65} = \\frac{-33}{65}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\cos(a + b)\\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{33}{65}}\\).'
'To find the vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\), we need to determine the direction vector of the line containing \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) such that the distance from \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) to any point \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) on the line is \\(t\\). This implies that \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) should be a unit vector in the direction from \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) to \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the direction vector from \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) to \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} -11 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 28 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -3 \\\\ -4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -11 - 5 \\\\ 1 + 3 \\\\ 28 + 4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -16 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 32 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nNext, find the magnitude of this direction vector:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{(-16)^2 + 4^2 + 32^2} = \\sqrt{256 + 16 + 1024} = \\sqrt{1296} = 36.\n\\]\n\nNow, normalize the direction vector to make it a unit vector:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{d} = \\frac{1}{36} \\begin{pmatrix} -16 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 32 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{16}{36} \\\\ \\frac{4}{36} \\\\ \\frac{32}{36} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{9} \\\\ \\frac{1}{9} \\\\ \\frac{8}{9} \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{9} \\\\ \\frac{1}{9} \\\\ \\frac{8}{9} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the vector that the dilation takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to, we first need to determine the scale factor of the dilation. The dilation takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -12 \\\\ -21 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe scale factor \\(k\\) can be found by comparing the components of the vectors:\n\n\\[\nk \\cdot 4 = -12 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad k = \\frac{-12}{4} = -3\n\\]\n\nWe can verify this with the second component:\n\n\\[\nk \\cdot 7 = -21 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad k = \\frac{-21}{7} = -3\n\\]\n\nThus, the scale factor is \\(-3\\).\n\nNow, we apply this scale factor to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\n\\[\nk \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} = -3 \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\cdot (-2) \\\\ -3 \\cdot 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\\\ -15 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the dilation takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\\\ -15 \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
'The projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto another vector \\(\\mathbf{w}\\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}}{\\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{w}\n\\]\n\nThe projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(-\\mathbf{w}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot (-\\mathbf{w})}{(-\\mathbf{w}) \\cdot (-\\mathbf{w})} (-\\mathbf{w})\n\\]\n\nSince \\((- \\mathbf{w}) \\cdot (- \\mathbf{w}) = \\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{-\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}}{\\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}} (-\\mathbf{w})\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = -\\left(\\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}}{\\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{w}\\right)\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = -\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v}\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -11 \\end{pmatrix}\\), it follows that:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = -\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -11 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 11 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(-\\mathbf{w}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 11 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the projection of vector \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto vector \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto another vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 8\\). First, we need to calculate \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = 2^2 + 6^2 + 3^2 = 4 + 36 + 9 = 49\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the projection:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{8}{49} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{16}{49} \\\\ \\frac{48}{49} \\\\ \\frac{24}{49} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the projection of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{16}{49} \\\\ \\frac{48}{49} \\\\ \\frac{24}{49} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To simplify the expression \\(\\frac{\\sec x}{\\sin x} - \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\\), we start by rewriting the trigonometric functions in terms of sine and cosine:\n\n1. Recall that \\(\\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}\\). Therefore, \\(\\frac{\\sec x}{\\sin x} = \\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\\).\n\n2. The second term is \\(\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\\).\n\nNow, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} - \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\n\\]\n\nTo combine these fractions, we need a common denominator. The common denominator is \\(\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x\\). Rewrite each term with this common denominator:\n\n- The first term is already \\(\\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\\).\n\n- Rewrite the second term:\n  \\[\n  \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{\\sin x \\cdot \\sin x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} = \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\n  \\]\n\nNow, the expression is:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} = \\frac{1 - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\n\\]\n\nRecall the Pythagorean identity: \\(1 - \\sin^2 x = \\cos^2 x\\). Substitute this into the expression:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\n\\]\n\nSimplify the fraction by canceling a \\(\\cos x\\) from the numerator and the denominator:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos x}{\\sin x}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\cot x\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{\\cot x}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\sin 5\\theta = \\sin^5 \\theta\\), we start by using the identity for \\(\\sin 5\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin 5\\theta = 16 \\sin^5 \\theta - 20 \\sin^3 \\theta + 5 \\sin \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSetting this equal to \\(\\sin^5 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n16 \\sin^5 \\theta - 20 \\sin^3 \\theta + 5 \\sin \\theta = \\sin^5 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we get:\n\n\\[\n15 \\sin^5 \\theta - 20 \\sin^3 \\theta + 5 \\sin \\theta = 0.\n\\]\n\nFactor out \\(\\sin \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta (15 \\sin^4 \\theta - 20 \\sin^2 \\theta + 5) = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two cases to consider:\n\n1. \\(\\sin \\theta = 0\\).\n2. \\(15 \\sin^4 \\theta - 20 \\sin^2 \\theta + 5 = 0\\).\n\nFor the first case, \\(\\sin \\theta = 0\\) implies \\(\\theta = 0\\), but \\(\\theta\\) is acute, so this is not possible.\n\nFor the second case, let \\(x = \\sin^2 \\theta\\). The equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n15x^2 - 20x + 5 = 0.\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 5:\n\n\\[\n3x^2 - 4x + 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSolve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} = \\frac{4 \\pm \\sqrt{16 - 12}}{6} = \\frac{4 \\pm 2}{6}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{6}{6} = 1 \\quad \\text{or} \\quad x = \\frac{2}{6} = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x = \\sin^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n- \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\) implies \\(\\sin \\theta = 1\\), which is not possible for an acute angle.\n- \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\\) implies \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\).\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\tan 2\\theta\\). We use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 2\\theta = \\frac{2 \\tan \\theta}{1 - \\tan^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, find \\(\\cos \\theta\\) using \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\frac{1}{3} = \\frac{2}{3} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThen, \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}/3}{\\sqrt{6}/3} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{6}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{6}} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\).\n\nNow, compute \\(\\tan 2\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan 2\\theta = \\frac{2 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}{1 - \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)^2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{1 - \\frac{1}{2}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{\\frac{1}{2}} = 2\\sqrt{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan 2\\theta = \\boxed{2\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
'To find the values of \\( k \\) for which the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a} + k \\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{b}\\) are orthogonal, we need to set their dot product to zero:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{a} + k \\mathbf{b}) \\cdot (\\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{b}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nExpanding the dot product, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + k \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - k^2 \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nSince the dot product is commutative, \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}\\), the expression simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 - k^2 \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given magnitudes \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = 3\\) and \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = 4\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n3^2 - k^2 \\cdot 4^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n9 - 16k^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( k^2 \\), we get:\n\n\\[\n16k^2 = 9 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad k^2 = \\frac{9}{16}.\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\nk = \\pm \\frac{3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the values of \\( k \\) for which the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a} + k \\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{b}\\) are orthogonal are \\(\\boxed{\\pm \\frac{3}{4}}\\).'
'To solve for \\(\\csc x + \\cot x\\) given that \\(\\sec x + \\tan x = \\frac{22}{7}\\), we start by expressing \\(\\sec x\\) and \\(\\tan x\\) in terms of sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}, \\quad \\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\n\\]\n\nThus, the given equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos x} + \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{22}{7}\n\\]\n\nCombining the terms on the left-hand side, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 + \\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{22}{7}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n7(1 + \\sin x) = 22 \\cos x\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we get:\n\n\\[\n7 + 7 \\sin x = 22 \\cos x\n\\]\n\nNext, we square both sides of the equation to eliminate the trigonometric functions:\n\n\\[\n(7 + 7 \\sin x)^2 = (22 \\cos x)^2\n\\]\n\nExpanding both sides, we have:\n\n\\[\n49 + 98 \\sin x + 49 \\sin^2 x = 484 \\cos^2 x\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\), we substitute:\n\n\\[\n49 + 98 \\sin x + 49 \\sin^2 x = 484 (1 - \\sin^2 x)\n\\]\n\nExpanding the right-hand side:\n\n\\[\n49 + 98 \\sin x + 49 \\sin^2 x = 484 - 484 \\sin^2 x\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms gives:\n\n\\[\n49 \\sin^2 x + 98 \\sin x + 49 = 484 - 484 \\sin^2 x\n\\]\n\nCombining like terms:\n\n\\[\n533 \\sin^2 x + 98 \\sin x - 435 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(\\sin x\\). Let \\(y = \\sin x\\), then:\n\n\\[\n533y^2 + 98y - 435 = 0\n\\]\n\nUsing the quadratic formula \\(y = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\\), where \\(a = 533\\), \\(b = 98\\), and \\(c = -435\\):\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-98 \\pm \\sqrt{98^2 - 4 \\cdot 533 \\cdot (-435)}}{2 \\cdot 533}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the discriminant:\n\n\\[\n98^2 = 9604, \\quad 4 \\cdot 533 \\cdot 435 = 926340\n\\]\n\n\\[\nb^2 - 4ac = 9604 + 926340 = 936944\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{936944} = 968\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions for \\(y\\) are:\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-98 \\pm 968}{1066}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the two possible values:\n\n1. \\(y = \\frac{-98 + 968}{1066} = \\frac{870}{1066} = \\frac{435}{533}\\)\n2. \\(y = \\frac{-98 - 968}{1066} = \\frac{-1066}{1066} = -1\\)\n\nSince \\(\\sin x = -1\\) is not possible for \\(\\sec x + \\tan x = \\frac{22}{7}\\), we use \\(\\sin x = \\frac{435}{533}\\).\n\nNow, we find \\(\\cos x\\) using \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\left(\\frac{435}{533}\\right)^2 = \\frac{533^2 - 435^2}{533^2}\n\\]\n\nCalculating:\n\n\\[\n533^2 = 284089, \\quad 435^2 = 189225\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 x = \\frac{284089 - 189225}{284089} = \\frac{94864}{284089}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos x = \\frac{\\sqrt{94864}}{533} = \\frac{308}{533}\n\\]\n\nNow, we find \\(\\csc x + \\cot x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\csc x = \\frac{1}{\\sin x} = \\frac{533}{435}, \\quad \\cot x = \\frac{\\cos x}{\\sin x} = \\'
'To compute the determinant of the \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 \\\\ 3 & 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the determinant of a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\det\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} = ad - bc\n\\]\n\nFor the given matrix, \\(a = 0\\), \\(b = 1\\), \\(c = 3\\), and \\(d = 5\\). Substituting these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\det\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 \\\\ 3 & 5 \\end{pmatrix} = (0)(5) - (1)(3) = 0 - 3 = -3\n\\]\n\nThus, the determinant of the matrix is \\(\\boxed{-3}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we need to compute the ratio of the sum of cosines to the sum of sines for angles from \\(1^\\circ\\) to \\(44^\\circ\\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\cos n^\\circ}{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\sin n^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nWe can use the identity for the sum of cosines and sines of an arithmetic sequence. The sum of cosines is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{N} \\cos n^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin\\left(\\frac{N \\cdot 1^\\circ}{2}\\right) \\cdot \\cos\\left(\\frac{(N+1) \\cdot 1^\\circ}{2}\\right)}{\\sin\\left(\\frac{1^\\circ}{2}\\right)}.\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, the sum of sines is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{N} \\sin n^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin\\left(\\frac{N \\cdot 1^\\circ}{2}\\right) \\cdot \\sin\\left(\\frac{(N+1) \\cdot 1^\\circ}{2}\\right)}{\\sin\\left(\\frac{1^\\circ}{2}\\right)}.\n\\]\n\nFor \\(N = 44\\), these become:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\cos n^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin(22^\\circ) \\cdot \\cos(22.5^\\circ)}{\\sin(0.5^\\circ)},\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\sin n^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin(22^\\circ) \\cdot \\sin(22.5^\\circ)}{\\sin(0.5^\\circ)}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\cos n^\\circ}{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\sin n^\\circ} = \\frac{\\cos(22.5^\\circ)}{\\sin(22.5^\\circ)} = \\cot(22.5^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nThe value of \\(\\cot(22.5^\\circ)\\) can be found using the half-angle identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(22.5^\\circ) = \\sqrt{\\frac{1 - \\cos(45^\\circ)}{1 + \\cos(45^\\circ)}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1 - \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}{1 + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this expression:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(22.5^\\circ) = \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{2}}{2 + \\sqrt{2}}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{(2 - \\sqrt{2})(2 - \\sqrt{2})}{(2 + \\sqrt{2})(2 - \\sqrt{2})}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{6 - 4\\sqrt{2}}{2}} = \\sqrt{2} - 1.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cot(22.5^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{\\tan(22.5^\\circ)} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2} - 1}\\).\n\nRationalizing the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\cot(22.5^\\circ) = \\frac{\\sqrt{2} + 1}{1} = \\sqrt{2} + 1.\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\sqrt{2} + 1}.\n\\]'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the line of reflection that maps \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The line of reflection is the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining these two points.\n\nFirst, find the midpoint of the segment joining \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\\[\n\\text{Midpoint} = \\left( \\frac{5 + 4}{2}, \\frac{0 + 3}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{9}{2}, \\frac{3}{2} \\right).\n\\]\n\nNext, find the direction vector of the line segment from \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\\[\n\\text{Direction vector} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 - 5 \\\\ 3 - 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe line of reflection is perpendicular to this direction vector, so its direction vector is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe equation of the line of reflection can be written in point-slope form using the midpoint \\(\\left( \\frac{9}{2}, \\frac{3}{2} \\right)\\):\n\\[\ny - \\frac{3}{2} = \\frac{1}{3} \\left( x - \\frac{9}{2} \\right).\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the image of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) under this reflection. The reflection of a point across a line can be found using the formula for reflection across a line in the form \\(ax + by + c = 0\\). The line of reflection can be rewritten as:\n\\[\n3x - y = \\frac{9}{2} - \\frac{3}{2} = 3.\n\\]\n\nThe line equation is \\(3x - y - 3 = 0\\).\n\nThe formula for the reflection of a point \\((x_1, y_1)\\) across the line \\(ax + by + c = 0\\) is:\n\\[\n(x', y') = \\left( x_1 - \\frac{2a(ax_1 + by_1 + c)}{a^2 + b^2}, y_1 - \\frac{2b(ax_1 + by_1 + c)}{a^2 + b^2} \\right).\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(a = 3\\), \\(b = -1\\), \\(c = -3\\), and \\((x_1, y_1) = (-2, 3)\\):\n\\[\nx' = -2 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 (3(-2) - 1(3) - 3)}{3^2 + (-1)^2},\n\\]\n\\[\ny' = 3 - \\frac{2 \\cdot (-1) (3(-2) - 1(3) - 3)}{3^2 + (-1)^2}.\n\\]\n\nCalculate:\n\\[\n3(-2) - 3 - 3 = -6 - 3 - 3 = -12,\n\\]\n\\[\nx' = -2 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot (-12)}{10} = -2 + \\frac{72}{10} = -2 + 7.2 = 5.2,\n\\]\n\\[\ny' = 3 - \\frac{2 \\cdot (-1) \\cdot (-12)}{10} = 3 - \\frac{24}{10} = 3 - 2.4 = 0.6.\n\\]\n\nThus, the reflection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5.2 \\\\ 0.6 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nTherefore, the reflection takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 5.2 \\\\ 0.6 \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
"To find the side length \\( s \\) of the cube, we need to determine the coordinates of the point \\( P = (x, y, z) \\) inside the cube that satisfy the given distances to the vertices.\n\nGiven:\n- \\( PA = \\sqrt{70} \\)\n- \\( PB = \\sqrt{97} \\)\n- \\( PC = \\sqrt{88} \\)\n- \\( PE = \\sqrt{43} \\)\n\nThe coordinates of the vertices are:\n- \\( A = (0, 0, 0) \\)\n- \\( B = (s, 0, 0) \\)\n- \\( C = (s, s, 0) \\)\n- \\( E = (0, 0, s) \\)\n\nUsing the distance formula, we have the following equations:\n\n1. \\( PA = \\sqrt{70} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{70} \\implies x^2 + y^2 + z^2 = 70\n   \\]\n\n2. \\( PB = \\sqrt{97} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{(x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{97} \\implies (x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2 = 97\n   \\]\n\n3. \\( PC = \\sqrt{88} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{(x-s)^2 + (y-s)^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{88} \\implies (x-s)^2 + (y-s)^2 + z^2 = 88\n   \\]\n\n4. \\( PE = \\sqrt{43} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2 + (z-s)^2} = \\sqrt{43} \\implies x^2 + y^2 + (z-s)^2 = 43\n   \\]\n\nNow, let's solve these equations step by step.\n\n**Step 1: Simplify the equations**\n\nFrom equation 1:\n\\[\nx^2 + y^2 + z^2 = 70\n\\]\n\nFrom equation 2:\n\\[\n(x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2 = 97 \\implies x^2 - 'To find the plane in which the line lies, we need to determine the values of \\(c\\) and \\(d\\) such that every point on the line satisfies the equation \\(x + y + cz = d\\).\n\nThe line is parameterized by:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 + t \\\\ 3t \\\\ 1 - t \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nThis gives us the coordinates:\n- \\(x = 1 + t\\)\n- \\(y = 3t\\)\n- \\(z = 1 - t\\)\n\nSubstitute these into the plane equation \\(x + y + cz = d\\):\n\\[\n(1 + t) + (3t) + c(1 - t) = d\n\\]\n\nSimplify the equation:\n\\[\n1 + t + 3t + c - ct = d\n\\]\n\\[\n1 + 4t + c - ct = d\n\\]\n\nRearrange terms:\n\\[\n(4 - c)t + (1 + c) = d\n\\]\n\nFor this equation to hold for all \\(t\\), the coefficient of \\(t\\) must be zero, and the constant term must equal \\(d\\). Therefore, we have:\n1. \\(4 - c = 0\\)\n2. \\(1 + c = d\\)\n\nFrom the first equation, solve for \\(c\\):\n\\[\nc = 4\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(c = 4\\) into the second equation to find \\(d\\):\n\\[\n1 + 4 = d \\implies d = 5\n\\]\n\nThus, the ordered pair \\((c, d)\\) is \\(\\boxed{(4, 5)}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the value of the expression\n\n\\[\n\\frac{AQ^2 + AR^2 + BP^2 + BR^2 + CP^2 + CQ^2}{AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2}.\n\\]\n\nWe start by considering the geometry of the problem. We have triangle \\(ABC\\) with midpoints \\(D\\), \\(E\\), and \\(F\\) of sides \\(\\overline{BC}\\), \\(\\overline{AC}\\), and \\(\\overline{AB}\\), respectively. Points \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are the midpoints of segments \\(\\overline{AD}\\), \\(\\overline{BE}\\), and \\(\\overline{CF}\\), respectively.\n\nSince \\(D\\), \\(E\\), and \\(F\\) are midpoints, the segments \\(\\overline{AD}\\), \\(\\overline{BE}\\), and \\(\\overline{CF}\\) are medians of the triangle. The points \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are the midpoints of these medians.\n\nBy the properties of medians and midpoints, the centroid \\(G\\) of the triangle \\(ABC\\) divides each median in the ratio \\(2:1\\). Therefore, \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are located at \\(\\frac{3}{4}\\) of the way from each vertex to the opposite side.\n\nNow, let's calculate the distances \\(AQ\\), \\(AR\\), \\(BP\\), \\(BR\\), \\(CP\\), and \\(CQ\\). Since \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are midpoints of the medians, they are also the centroids of the smaller triangles formed by the medians. Thus, each of these distances is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\) of the length of the corresponding median.\n\nThe length of a median in a triangle can be calculated using the formula:\n\n\\[\nm_a = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) are the sides of the triangle, and \\(m_a\\) is the median to side \\(a\\).\n\nFor triangle \\(ABC\\), the medians are:\n\n- \\(m_a\\) to side \\(BC\\),\n- \\(m_b\\) to side \\(AC\\),\n- \\(m_c\\) to side \\(AB\\).\n\nThe distances from the centroid to the vertices are \\(\\frac{2}{3}\\) of the medians, so the distances from the midpoints of the medians to the vertices are \\(\\frac{1}{3}\\) of the medians.\n\nThus, the distances \\(AQ\\), \\(AR\\), \\(BP\\), \\(BR\\), \\(CP\\), and \\(CQ\\) are each \\(\\frac{1}{3}\\) of the corresponding median lengths.\n\nNow, we calculate the sum of the squares of these distances:\n\n\\[\nAQ^2 + AR^2 + BP^2 + BR^2 + CP^2 + CQ^2 = 6 \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^2 \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2 \\times (AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n= 6 \\times \\frac{1}{9} \\times \\frac{1}{4} \\times (AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2) = \\frac{1}{6} \\times (AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2).\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\frac{1}{6} \\times (AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2)}{AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2} = \\frac{1}{6}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{6}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to use properties of determinants. We are given that:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ g & h & i \\end{vmatrix} = -7\n\\]\n\nWe need to find the determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe can use the linearity property of determinants, which states that the determinant of a matrix is linear in each row. Specifically, if a row of a determinant is a linear combination of other rows, we can split the determinant into the sum of determinants with each row separately.\n\nFirst, let's consider the second row of the matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis row can be expressed as a linear combination of the second and third rows of the original matrix:\n\n\\[\n2 \\begin{pmatrix} d & e & f \\end{pmatrix} + 5 \\begin{pmatrix} g & h & i \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nUsing the linearity of determinants, we can split the determinant as follows:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix} = 2 \\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix} + 5 \\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ g & h & i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, let's evaluate each of these determinants:\n\n1. For the first determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe can factor out a \\(-1\\) from the third row:\n\n\\[\n= -\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ g & h & i \\end{vmatrix} = -(-7) = 7\n\\]\n\n2. For the second determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ g & h & i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNotice that the second and third rows are negatives of each other, which means the determinant is zero because two rows are linearly dependent:\n\n\\[\n= 0\n\\]\n\nPutting it all together, we have:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cdot 7 + 5 \\cdot 0 = 14 + 0 = 14\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the determinant is \\(\\boxed{14}\\)."

'To find the area of triangle \\( OAB \\), we can use the cross product of the vectors \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{OB}\\). The area of the triangle is half the magnitude of the cross product of these two vectors.\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{OA} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}, \\quad \\overrightarrow{OB} = \\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\\\ -2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe cross product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\times \\overrightarrow{OB}\\) is calculated as follows:\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{OA} \\times \\overrightarrow{OB} = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 1 & 2 & 3 \\\\ -3 & -2 & 1 \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nExpanding the determinant, we have:\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i} \\left( 2 \\cdot 1 - 3 \\cdot (-2) \\right) - \\mathbf{j} \\left( 1 \\cdot 1 - 3 \\cdot (-3) \\right) + \\mathbf{k} \\left( 1 \\cdot (-2) - 2 \\cdot (-3) \\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i} (2 + 6) - \\mathbf{j} (1 + 9) + \\mathbf{k} (-2 + 6)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i} \\cdot 8 - \\mathbf{j} \\cdot 10 + \\mathbf{k} \\cdot 4\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ -10 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this vector is:\n\\[\n\\left\\| \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ -10 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} \\right\\| = \\sqrt{8^2 + (-10)^2 + 4^2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{64 + 100 + 16}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{180}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{36 \\times 5} = 6\\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nThe area of triangle \\( OAB \\) is half the magnitude of the cross product:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 6\\sqrt{5} = 3\\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of triangle \\( OAB \\) is \\(\\boxed{3\\sqrt{5}}\\).'

'To find the reflection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over another vector \\(\\mathbf{u} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we first need to find the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{u}\\). The formula for the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{u}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{u}}{\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{u}\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate the dot products:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{u} = (-2)(2) + (3)(1) = -4 + 3 = -1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{u} = (2)(2) + (1)(1) = 4 + 1 = 5\n\\]\n\nNow, compute the projection:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{-1}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{2}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{1}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe reflection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) over \\(\\mathbf{u}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Reflection} = 2 \\cdot \\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{v}\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\(2 \\cdot \\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{v}\\):\n\n\\[\n2 \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{2}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{1}{5} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, find the reflection:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Reflection} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} + 2 \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} - 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSimplify the components:\n\n\\[\n= \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} + \\frac{10}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} - \\frac{15}{5} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{6}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{17}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the reflection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{6}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{17}{5} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find \\(\\sin L\\) in triangle \\(LMN\\), we start by using the Pythagorean theorem. Given that \\(\\angle M = 90^\\circ\\), \\(MN = 2\\), and \\(LM = \\sqrt{21}\\), we can find the length of the hypotenuse \\(LN\\).\n\nThe Pythagorean theorem states:\n\\[\nLM^2 + MN^2 = LN^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given values:\n\\[\n(\\sqrt{21})^2 + 2^2 = LN^2\n\\]\n\nCalculating the squares:\n\\[\n21 + 4 = LN^2\n\\]\n\nAdding the values:\n\\[\n25 = LN^2\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides:\n\\[\nLN = \\sqrt{25} = 5\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find \\(\\sin L\\). In a right triangle, \\(\\sin L\\) is the ratio of the length of the side opposite angle \\(L\\) to the length of the hypotenuse. Here, the side opposite \\(\\angle L\\) is \\(MN\\), and the hypotenuse is \\(LN\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\sin L\\) is:\n\\[\n\\sin L = \\frac{MN}{LN} = \\frac{2}{5}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\sin L = \\boxed{\\frac{2}{5}}\\).'
'To find the coordinates of the orthocenter \\( H \\) in terms of the vertices \\( A \\), \\( B \\), and \\( C \\), we use the fact that the orthocenter can be expressed as a linear combination of the vertices:\n\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{H} = x \\overrightarrow{A} + y \\overrightarrow{B} + z \\overrightarrow{C}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( x + y + z = 1 \\).\n\nIn a triangle, the orthocenter can be expressed using the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{H} = \\frac{\\tan A \\cdot \\overrightarrow{A} + \\tan B \\cdot \\overrightarrow{B} + \\tan C \\cdot \\overrightarrow{C}}{\\tan A + \\tan B + \\tan C}\n\\]\n\nHowever, we can also use the fact that the orthocenter is the intersection of the altitudes. For a triangle with sides \\( a = 8 \\), \\( b = 7 \\), and \\( c = 5 \\), we can use the following properties:\n\n- The orthocenter \\( H \\) is the intersection of the altitudes.\n- The altitudes are perpendicular to the opposite sides.\n\nTo find the coefficients \\( x \\), \\( y \\), and \\( z \\), we use the fact that the orthocenter can be expressed in terms of the angles of the triangle:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{\\tan A}{\\tan A + \\tan B + \\tan C}, \\quad y = \\frac{\\tan B}{\\tan A + \\tan B + \\tan C}, \\quad z = \\frac{\\tan C}{\\tan A + \\tan B + \\tan C}\n\\]\n\nHowever, a more straightforward approach is to use the known result for the orthocenter in terms of the side lengths:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{a^2(b^2 + c^2 - a^2)}{(a^2 + b^2 - c^2)(a^2 + c^2 - b^2)}, \\quad y = \\frac{b^2(a^2 + c^2 - b^2)}{(a^2 + b^2 - c^2)(b^2 + c^2 - a^2)}, \\quad z = \\frac{c^2(a^2 + b^2 - c^2)}{(b^2 + c^2 - a^2)(a^2 + c^2 - b^2)}\n\\]\n\nFor triangle \\( ABC \\) with sides \\( a = 8 \\), \\( b = 7 \\), and \\( c = 5 \\), we calculate:\n\n- \\( a^2 = 64 \\), \\( b^2 = 49 \\), \\( c^2 = 25 \\)\n- \\( b^2 + c^2 - a^2 = 49 + 25 - 64 = 10 \\)\n- \\( a^2 + c^2 - b^2 = 64 + 25 - 49 = 40 \\)\n- \\( a^2 + b^2 - c^2 = 64 + 49 - 25 = 88 \\)\n\nNow, calculate the coefficients:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{64 \\times 10}{88 \\times 40} = \\frac{640}{3520} = \\frac{1}{5.5}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{49 \\times 40}{88 \\times 10} = \\frac{1960}{880} = \\frac{2.227}{5.5}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nz = \\frac{25 \\times 88}{10 \\times 40} = \\frac{2200}{400} = \\frac{5.5}{5.5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the ordered triple is:\n\n\\[\n(x, y, z) = \\left(\\frac{1}{5.5}, \\frac{2.227}{5.5}, \\frac{5.5}{5.5}\\right)\n\\]\n\nHowever, simplifying these values, we find:\n\n\\[\n(x, y, z) = \\left(\\frac{1}{5.5}, \\frac{2.227}{5.5}, \\frac{5.5}{5.5}\\right) = \\left(\\frac{1}{5.5}, \\frac{2.227}{5.5}, 1\\right)\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n(x, y, z) = \\left(\\frac{1}{5.5}, \\frac{2.227}{5.5}, 1\\right)\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the ordered triple is \\(\\boxed{\\left(\\frac{1}{5.5}, \\frac{2.227}{5.5}, 1\\right)}\\).'
'To find \\(\\det (-3\\mathbf{A})\\), we can use the properties of determinants. Specifically, if \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix and \\(c\\) is a scalar, then:\n\n\\[\n\\det (c\\mathbf{A}) = c^n \\det (\\mathbf{A})\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(n\\) is the size of the matrix, which in this case is 2. Therefore, for \\(-3\\mathbf{A}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\det (-3\\mathbf{A}) = (-3)^2 \\det (\\mathbf{A})\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\det (\\mathbf{A}) = 2\\), we can substitute this into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\det (-3\\mathbf{A}) = 9 \\times 2 = 18\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\det (-3\\mathbf{A}) = \\boxed{18}\\).'
'To find the reflection of the point \\((3,6,15)\\) across the line \\(L\\) that passes through the origin and the point \\((2,1,-2)\\), we first need to determine the direction vector of the line \\(L\\). The direction vector is \\(\\mathbf{d} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe reflection of a point \\(\\mathbf{p}\\) across a line in space can be found using the formula:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{p}_{\\text{reflected}} = 2 \\cdot \\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{d}} \\mathbf{p} - \\mathbf{p}\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{d}} \\mathbf{p}\\) is the projection of \\(\\mathbf{p}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{d}\\).\n\nFirst, we calculate the projection of \\(\\mathbf{p} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 15 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{d} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{d}} \\mathbf{p} = \\frac{\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{d}}{\\mathbf{d} \\cdot \\mathbf{d}} \\mathbf{d}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the dot products:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{d} = 3 \\cdot 2 + 6 \\cdot 1 + 15 \\cdot (-2) = 6 + 6 - 30 = -18\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{d} \\cdot \\mathbf{d} = 2 \\cdot 2 + 1 \\cdot 1 + (-2) \\cdot (-2) = 4 + 1 + 4 = 9\n\\]\n\nThus, the projection is:\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{d}} \\mathbf{p} = \\frac{-18}{9} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} = -2 \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -4 \\\\ -2 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, use the reflection formula:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{p}_{\\text{reflected}} = 2 \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -4 \\\\ -2 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 15 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -8 \\\\ -4 \\\\ 8 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 15 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -11 \\\\ -10 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the reflection of the point \\((3,6,15)\\) across the line \\(L\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -11 \\\\ -10 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we first need to convert the given rectangular coordinates \\((2, -1, -2)\\) to spherical coordinates \\((\\rho, \\theta, \\phi)\\).\n\n1. **Calculate \\(\\rho\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\rho = \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{2^2 + (-1)^2 + (-2)^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 1 + 4} = \\sqrt{9} = 3.\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate \\(\\theta\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{y}{x}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-1}{2}\\right).\n   \\]\n   This gives \\(\\theta\\) in the correct quadrant since \\(x = 2 > 0\\).\n\n3. **Calculate \\(\\phi\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\phi = \\cos^{-1}\\left(\\frac{z}{\\rho}\\right) = \\cos^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-2}{3}\\right).\n   \\]\n\nNow, we need to find the rectangular coordinates of the point with spherical coordinates \\((\\rho, \\theta, 2\\phi)\\).\n\n1. **New \\(\\phi\\):**\n   \\[\n   2\\phi = 2 \\cdot \\cos^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-2}{3}\\right).\n   \\]\n\n2. **Convert back to rectangular coordinates:**\n   \\[\n   x' = \\rho \\sin(2\\phi) \\cos(\\theta),\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   y' = \\rho \\sin(2\\phi) \\sin(\\theta),\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   z' = \\rho \\cos(2\\phi).\n   \\]\n\n   Using the identity \\(\\cos(2\\phi) = 2\\cos^2(\\phi) - 1\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\cos(2\\phi) = 2\\left(\\frac{-2}{3}\\right)^2 - 1 = 2\\left(\\frac{4}{9}\\right) - 1 = \\frac{8}{9} - 1 = -\\frac{1}{9}.\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   z' = \\rho \\cos(2\\phi) = 3 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{9}\\right) = -\\frac{1}{3}.\n   \\]\n\n   Using the identity \\(\\sin(2\\phi) = 2\\sin(\\phi)\\cos(\\phi)\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin(\\phi) = \\sqrt{1 - \\cos^2(\\phi)} = \\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{-2}{3}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{1 - \\frac{4}{9}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{5}{9}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{5}}{3}.\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sin(2\\phi) = 2 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{5}}{3} \\cdot \\frac{-2}{3} = -\\frac{4\\sqrt{5}}{9}.\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   x' = \\rho \\sin(2\\phi) \\cos(\\theta) = 3 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{4\\sqrt{5}}{9}\\right) \\cdot \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{5}} = -\\frac{8}{3},\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   y' = \\rho \\sin(2\\phi) \\sin(\\theta) = 3 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{4\\sqrt{5}}{9}\\right) \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{2\\sqrt{5}}\\right) = \\frac{2}{3}.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the rectangular coordinates of the point with spherical coordinates \\((\\rho, \\theta, 2\\phi)\\) are \\(\\left(-\\frac{8}{3}, \\frac{2}{3}, -\\frac{1}{3}\\right)\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to analyze the given equation in the context of triangle \\(ABC\\). The equation is:\n\n\\[ a^4 + b^4 + c^4 = 2c^2 (a^2 + b^2). \\]\n\nWe can start by expanding the right-hand side:\n\n\\[ 2c^2(a^2 + b^2) = 2c^2a^2 + 2c^2b^2. \\]\n\nRewriting the equation, we have:\n\n\\[ a^4 + b^4 + c^4 = 2c^2a^2 + 2c^2b^2. \\]\n\nRearranging terms gives:\n\n\\[ a^4 + b^4 + c^4 - 2c^2a^2 - 2c^2b^2 = 0. \\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[ a^4 - 2c^2a^2 + b^4 - 2c^2b^2 + c^4 = 0. \\]\n\nNotice that the terms \\(a^4 - 2c^2a^2\\) and \\(b^4 - 2c^2b^2\\) can be factored as:\n\n\\[ (a^2 - c^2)^2 - c^4 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad (b^2 - c^2)^2 - c^4. \\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[ (a^2 - c^2)^2 + (b^2 - c^2)^2 + c^4 = 0. \\]\n\nFor this equation to hold, each squared term must be zero, because squares of real numbers are non-negative. Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[ a^2 - c^2 = 0 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad b^2 - c^2 = 0. \\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[ a^2 = c^2 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad b^2 = c^2. \\]\n\nThus, \\(a = c\\) and \\(b = c\\). Therefore, triangle \\(ABC\\) is an equilateral triangle, and \\(\\angle C = 60^\\circ\\).\n\nHowever, we should also consider the possibility of a degenerate triangle where one of the angles is \\(180^\\circ\\). In such a case, the triangle would collapse into a line, and the equation would still hold. Therefore, \\(\\angle C\\) could also be \\(180^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the possible values of \\(\\angle C\\) are \\(\\boxed{60, 180}\\).'
'To simplify the expression \\(\\frac{\\sin^4 x + \\cos^4 x - 1}{\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x - 1}\\), we start by using some trigonometric identities.\n\nFirst, note that:\n\\[\n\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x = 1.\n\\]\n\nWe can express \\(\\sin^4 x + \\cos^4 x\\) in terms of \\(\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x\\):\n\\[\n\\sin^4 x + \\cos^4 x = (\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x)^2 - 2\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x = 1 - 2\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x.\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for \\(\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x\\), we use the identity:\n\\[\n\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x = (\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x)(\\sin^4 x - \\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x + \\cos^4 x).\n\\]\nSince \\(\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x = 1\\), this simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x = \\sin^4 x - \\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x + \\cos^4 x.\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\(\\sin^4 x + \\cos^4 x = 1 - 2\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x\\) into the expression for \\(\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x\\):\n\\[\n\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x = (1 - 2\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x) - \\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x = 1 - 3\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x.\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these into the original expression:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^4 x + \\cos^4 x - 1}{\\sin^6 x + \\cos^6 x - 1} = \\frac{(1 - 2\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x) - 1}{(1 - 3\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x) - 1}.\n\\]\n\nSimplify the numerator and the denominator:\n\\[\n= \\frac{-2\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x}{-3\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nCancel out the common factor \\(-\\sin^2 x \\cos^2 x\\) from the numerator and the denominator:\n\\[\n= \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{3}}\\).'
